The Fourth Objection: Impossibility as a Middle State
Razi presents the fourth objection: impossibility as a middle state between existence and nonexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of impossibility is used to argue for a middle state between existence and nonexistence, challenging the principle of excluded middle.

Core Idea
Impossibility is neither existent nor nonexistent, forming a middle state.

The Mu'tazila objected to the principle that a thing is either existent or nonexistent by pointing to impossibility (al-imitināʿ). They argued that impossibility cannot be classified as either existent (since its bearer would then be existent, making it possible or necessary) or nonexistent (since it is a distinct quiddity, not a pure negation). This passage presents that argument and defends the existence of a middle state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow and the 'two aspects' mentioned at the start.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage begins by stating 'from two aspects' (min wajhayn). The first aspect is the main argument that impossibility cannot be existent or nonexistent. The second aspect is implied in the rebuttal about mental existence. Locate these parts.
The 'two aspects' refer to the core dilemma and the response to the rebuttal.
3
Analyze the core dilemma
Focus on the first part: if impossibility is existent, its bearer would be existent (making the impossible possible or necessary); if nonexistent, then impossibility would be a negation of a negation, contradicting its status as a distinct quiddity. Understand why both options are rejected.
Key terms: موجود, معدوم, امتناع, ماهية.
4
Evaluate the rebuttal about mental existence
The objector might say impossibility exists in the mind. Razi offers three responses: impossibility is impossible in itself regardless of mind; mental supposition must correspond to external reality; and mental existence cannot bear the attribute of impossibility. Trace these responses.
This strengthens the claim that impossibility is neither existent nor nonexistent.
5
Conclude with the middle state
The argument concludes that the meaning of impossibility is neither existent nor nonexistent, thus proving a middle state (al-wāsiṭa). Reflect on how this challenges the principle of excluded middle.
This objection is a key argument for the possibility of a middle state in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الرابع على قبولنا الشيء إما أن يكون وإما أن لا يكون : سلمنا تصور هذه القضية بأجزائها لا نسلم عدم الواسطة وبيانه من وجهين . الأول : أن مسمى الامتناع إما أن يكون موجودا أو معدوما لا جائز أن يكون موجودا وإلا لكان الموصوف به موجودا لاستحالة قيام الموجود بالمعدوم ولو كان الموصوف به موجودا لم يكن الممتنع ممتنعا ، بل إما واجبا أو ممكنا، ولا جائز أن يكون معدوما لأنه نقيض الامتناع الذي يمكن حمله على المعدوم فيكون اللاامتناع عدميا فلا يكون الامتناع عدميا . ولأن الامتناع ماهية متعينة في نفسها متميزة من سائر الماهيات إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لاستحال إشارة العقل إليها ، وإذا كان كذلك استحال أن يكون نفيا محضا . فإن قلت : ثبوت في الذهن. قلت هذا باطل لأن الممتنع ممتنع في نفسه سواء كان هناك عقل أو لم يكن ولأن الفرض العقلي إن كان مطابقا للخارج فهو المطلوب وإلا لكان كاذبا وليس كلامنا فيه بل فيما يطابق الموجود، ولأن الذي في الذهن إن كان موجودا استحال اتصافه بالامتناع لأن الموجود لا يكون ممتنع الوجود وإن لم يكن موجودا لم يكن الامتناع القائم به موجودا لاستحالة قيام الموجود بما ليس بموجود؛ فثبت أن مسمى الامتناع ليس بموجود ولا معدوم وذلك هو الواسطة .
The fourth objection against our acceptance of 'a thing is either existent or not': We concede the conception of this proposition in its parts, but we do not concede the absence of a middle. Its demonstration is from two aspects. First: the meaning of impossibility (al-imitināʿ) is either existent or nonexistent. It cannot be existent because then its bearer would be existent, since it is impossible for an existent to subsist in a nonexistent. And if its bearer were existent, the impossible would not be impossible, but rather either necessary or possible. And it cannot be nonexistent because it is the contradicting term of non-impossibility (al-lāimitināʿ) which can be predicated of the nonexistent, so non-impossibility would be negative, and thus impossibility would not be negative. And because impossibility is a quiddity (māhiyya) determined in itself, distinct from other quiddities; if it were not so, the mind's reference to it would be impossible. If that is the case, it is impossible for it to be a pure negation. If you say: it is established in the mind. We say: this is false because the impossible is impossible in itself whether there is a mind or not; and because the rational supposition, if it corresponds to the external, is what is required, otherwise it would be false, and our discussion is not about it but about what corresponds to the existent. And because what is in the mind, if it is existent, it is impossible for it to be described by impossibility because the existent cannot be impossible of existence; and if it is not existent, then the impossibility subsisting in it is not existent because it is impossible for an existent to subsist in what is not existent. So it is established that the meaning of impossibility is neither existent nor nonexistent, and that is the middle.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection against the principle of excluded middle. The opponent argues that there is a middle between existence and nonexistence, using impossibility (al-imitināʿ) as an example. The argument proceeds: impossibility must be either existent or nonexistent. If existent, then the substrate (the impossible thing) would be existent, but then it would not be impossible (since impossible things cannot subsist). If nonexistent, then impossibility would be a negation of a negation (since its contradictory 'non-impossibility' can be predicated of nonexistent things, making it negative, so impossibility would be positive). Yet impossibility is a distinct conceived quiddity, not a pure negation. A possible rebuttal that impossibility is mental is dismissed: the impossible is impossible regardless of mind; mental correspondence to reality is required; and mental existence cannot bear impossibility (if existent, it cannot be impossible; if nonexistent, it cannot be a bearer). Therefore impossibility is neither existent nor nonexistent, proving a middle.

Key Terms
امتناع (impossibility)
ماهية (quiddity)
نفي محض (pure negation)
الواسطة (the middle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impossibility is merely a mental concept that exists in the mind.
Correction: Razi argues that impossibility is a real quiddity that does not depend on the mind; the impossible is impossible in itself, and mental existence cannot bear impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth objection, why cannot impossibility be nonexistent?
A) Because nonexistent things cannot have attributes.
B) Because non-impossibility would then be negative, making impossibility positive, and impossibility is a distinct quiddity.
C) Because impossibility must be existent by definition.
D) Because the mind cannot conceive of impossibility.

Key Takeaway
Impossibility is neither existent nor nonexistent, serving as a counterexample to the principle of excluded middle and asserting a middle state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that impossibility is a real quiddity, not just a mental construct. Think of a sin you have convinced yourself is 'impossible' for you to commit — not because of external constraints, but because of your own perceived nature. Is that judgment based on a genuine impossibility, or on a comfortable assumption that protects you from accountability? Write down one specific action you will stop labeling 'impossible' until you have examined it with the same logical rigor as this passage.
?
The passage teaches that what is in the mind must correspond to external reality. Consider a belief you hold about yourself — for example, 'I cannot overcome this bad habit' or 'I am not capable of this good deed'. Is that belief just a mental construct, or does it reflect a true impossibility? If it fails to correspond to reality, what will you do today to reject that false supposition and act against it?
?
Impossibility is described as a distinct quiddity that is neither existent nor nonexistent. How does this change your understanding of the attributes of Allah? Specifically, think about 'He is not like anything' — does this mean impossibility is a kind of likeness? Or does it show that there are real concepts beyond existence and nonexistence that help you grasp tawḥīd? Sit with the discomfort of not knowing and ask Allah to increase your understanding.

Temporality: A Middle State Between Existence and Nonexistence
Razi argues that the meaning of temporality is a middle between existence and nonexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the quiddity at the moment of temporality cannot be described as either existent or nonexistent, thereby requiring a third category.

Core Idea
Temporality is a state that is neither existence nor nonexistence.

Imam Razi presents a logical argument to demonstrate that the meaning of temporality cannot be reduced to existence or nonexistence. He examines the condition of the quiddity at the exact moment of coming into existence and shows that both options lead to contradictions, forcing us to accept a middle state. This lesson will walk through his reasoning and its significance for ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the formal structure starting with 'وثانيهما' indicating this is the second proof.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins by asserting that the meaning of temporality is distinct from both existence and nonexistence. Understand why this is necessary.
3
Examine the dilemma at the moment
The moment of temporality (al-ān) is introduced. The quiddity must be either existent, nonexistent, or neither. Follow the refutation of each possibility.
Focus on the two impossibilities: existence leads to duplication, nonexistence prevents change.
4
Analyze the impossibility of existence
If the quiddity were existent at that moment, it would be like saying 'the existent emerges to existence', implying a double existence, which is absurd.
5
Analyze the impossibility of nonexistence
If it were nonexistent, two problems arise: temporality as an existent attribute cannot inhere in a nonexistent, and the original nonexistence would persist, preventing any change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما : أن مسمى الحدوث وهو الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود غير مسمى العدم ومسمى الوجود وإلا لكان حيث صدق مسمى العدم أو مسمى الوجود صدق مسمى الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود وهو محال، وإذا ثبت ذاك فنقول : الآن الذي يصدق فيه على الماهية مسمى الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود إما أن تكون الماهية في ذلك الآن موجودة أو معدومة أو لا موجودة ولا معدومة فإن كانت موجودة فقد صدق على الموجود أنه يخرج من العدم إلى الوجود فيكون ذلك كأنه يقال الموجود يخرج إلى الوجود فيكون الشيء موجودا مرتين وهو محال وإن كانت معدومة فهو محال من وجهين . أحدهما : أنه إذا كانت معدومة كانت باقية على العدم الأصلي ومع البقاء على العدم الأصلي يستحيل أن يتحقق مسمى الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود والحدوث صفة موجودة والصفة الموجودة يستحيل قيامها بالمعدوم . الثاني : متى كانت معدومة كان القدم الأصلي باقيا ومتى كان العدم الأصلي باقيا لم يكن النقل في التغير من العدم حاصلا؛ فثبت أن الماهية حالة الحدوث لا موجودة ولا معدومة .
Secondly: the meaning of temporality (al-ḥudūth), which is the emergence from nonexistence to existence, is distinct from the meaning of nonexistence and the meaning of existence; otherwise, wherever the meaning of nonexistence or the meaning of existence is true, the meaning of emergence from nonexistence to existence would be true, which is impossible. And if that is established, we say: the moment (al-ān) in which the meaning of emergence from nonexistence to existence is true of the quiddity (al-māhiyya), then either the quiddity is existent at that moment, or nonexistent, or neither existent nor nonexistent. If it is existent, then it would be true of the existent that it emerges from nonexistence to existence, which is like saying 'the existent emerges to existence', so the thing would be existent twice, which is impossible. If it is nonexistent, it is impossible from two aspects. First: if it is nonexistent, it remains in the original nonexistence, and with remaining in the original nonexistence, it is impossible for the meaning of emergence from nonexistence to existence to be realized; also, temporality is an existent attribute, and an existent attribute cannot subsist in a nonexistent. Second: when it is nonexistent, the original pre-eternity (al-qidam) remains, and when the original nonexistence remains, the transfer in change from nonexistence does not occur. So it is established that the quiddity at the moment of temporality is neither existent nor nonexistent.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of the fourth objection, using temporality (al-ḥudūth) as an example of a middle. First, it is argued that the meaning of temporality is distinct from both existence and nonexistence; otherwise, the meaning of temporality would coincide with whichever predicate is true, which is absurd. Then, the state of the quiddity (essence) at the moment of coming into existence is examined. It cannot be existent because that would mean an existent emerges to existence — a duplication. It cannot be nonexistent because then it would remain in original nonexistence, and temporality as an existent attribute cannot inhere in a nonexistent; also, the original pre-eternity (qidam) would remain, preventing any change. Therefore the quiddity at that moment is neither existent nor nonexistent, confirming a middle state.

Key Terms
حدوث
خروج من العدم إلى الوجود
آن
ماهية
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moment of coming into existence is simply when the thing becomes existent, so it is existent at that moment.
Correction: Razi argues that if it were existent at that moment, it would be existent twice, which is absurd. Instead, the quiddity is in a middle state.

Checkpoint
Why can't the quiddity be existent at the moment of temporality?
A) Because temporality is a nonexistent attribute.
B) Because it would mean the existent emerges to existence, which is a duplication.
C) Because the quiddity must be nonexistent to emerge.
D) Because existence is eternal.

Key Takeaway
At the moment of temporality, the quiddity is neither existent nor nonexistent, illustrating a third rational category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a moment in your life when you felt you were in a state of transition—between what you were and what you are becoming. Are you content to exist in that ambiguous state, or do you demand clarity? Razi shows that even the moment of coming into existence defies easy categorization. How does this affect your understanding of your own moments of change?
?
The quiddity at the moment of temporality is neither existent nor nonexistent. This is not a logical trick but a profound truth about the nature of coming to be. When you reflect on your own existence, do you ever consider that your being is not a simple yes or no? How might recognizing the complexity of your own coming into being change your gratitude toward the One who brought you from nothing?
?
Razi argues that the moment of temporality cannot be described by the categories we normally use. Are you comfortable with realities that elude simple classification? In your relationship with Allah, are there aspects of your faith that cannot be neatly categorized—like hope and fear, or certainty and doubt? How do you hold them together without forcing them into one box?

Refuting the Objection of a Middle State During Transition
Imam Razi presents and refutes the objection of a middle state during transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a middle state between existence and non-existence is impossible and how Imam Razi refutes the argument from transition.

Core Idea
No real intermediate state exists between existence and non-existence; transition is only conceptual.

Imam Razi presents an objection that the process of a quiddity moving from non-existence to existence seems to require a middle state. He then refutes this by showing that transition from non-existence is not a gradual process, and any intermediary would be contradictory. This lesson covers the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection argues that during transition, the quiddity must be neither purely non-existent nor purely existent. Note the logical structure.
Focus on the dilemma: if non-existent, no transition; if existent, transition is complete.
2
Study the refutation in the author's comment
Imam Razi's own comment (أقول) explains that transition from nothing does not involve a real intermediate because the starting point (non-existence) is not established.
The refutation hinges on the nature of transition: it requires a gradual process, which does not apply when moving from nothing.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The refutation demonstrates that the law of excluded middle remains intact; no middle state exists.
This reinforces the foundational principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله تقرير آخر وهو أن الماهية إذا انتقلت من العدم إلى الوجود فعال الانتقال لابد وأن تكون لا معدومة ولا موجودة؛ لأنها لو كانت معدومة فهي بعد لم تأخذ في الانتقال بل هي باقية كما كانت قبل ذلك، ولو كانت موجودة فقد حصل المنتقل إليها، وحين حصول المنتقل إليها بتمامه لم يبق الانتقال بل ينقطع، فظاهر أن حصول الانتقال لابد وأن يكون متوسطا بين المنتقل عنه والمنتقل إليه، فوجب أن يكون خارجا عن حد العدم الصرف وغير داخل إلى حد الوجود الصرف (1)

فهذه الإشكالات قطرة من بحار الإشكالات الواردة على قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون وأما أن لا يكون، وإذا كان حال أقوى البديهيات كذلك فما ظنك بالأضعف (2)

الحجة الثانية: لمنكري البديهيات: أنا نجد العقل جازما بأمور كثيرة بجزمه بالأوليات مع أن الجزم غير جائز فيها، وذلك يوجب تطرق التهمة إلى حكم العقل.

بيان الأول من وجوه.
..
شأنه
ب الوجود والعدم ونسبة بينهما ولا شيء مما يدخل في مفهومه العدم والنسبة إليه موجودة في الخارج، والحدوث معنى معقول هو صفة تحصل في العقل عند تعقل العدم، والوجود المرتب عليه في الفعل والماهية الموصوفة بتلك الصفة لا تكون موصوفة بالوجود وحده فلا تكون موجودة في الخارج بل إنما تكون موجودة في العقل، ولا يلزم من ذلك واسطة بين الوجود والعدم؛ لأن معنى الواسطة أن تكون الماهية في الخارج غير موصوفة بالوجود ولا بالعدم وذلك محال؛ لأن كون الماهية في الخارج هو وجودها الخارجي فكونها في الخارج غير موصوفة بالوجود تناقض. لا يقال الجسم في آن انتقاله من المكون إلى الحركة موجود وليس بموصوف بالسكون ولا بالحركة ولا يمكن أن يقال: الجسم في ذلك الآن موجود في العقل فقط فإذا هو في ذلك الزمان لا ساكن ولا متحرك ويلزم منه واسطة بين السكون والحركة المتقابلين. لانا نقول: وجود الحركة لا يمكن إلا في زمان، وكذلك وجود السكون، وانتفاؤهما عن شيء من أن يوجد أحدهما فيه يقتضي واسطة بينهما، لكن الجسم في الآن الذي هو الفعل المشترك بين زمان المسكون وزمان الحركة لا يكون من شأنه أن يوجد فيه حركة أو سكون، فلا يلزم من وجوده في ذلك الآن ثبوت واسطة بين الحركة والسكون بكون الجسم موصوفا بهما في ذلك الآن، وهذا بخلاف ما نحن فيه، لأن الماهية في الآن الذي لا تكون موصوفة بالوجود المحض لا يكون ثابتة، فإن ثبوتها في حال اتصافها بالوجود فقط. (1) أقول: الأخذ في الانتقال وانقطاع الانتقال لا يصحان إلا إذا كان الانتقال واقعا في شيء موجود بالتدريج كالحركة، أما إذا كان الانتقال من لا شيء فلا يكون هناك حد ولا انقطاع، والمتوسط بين المنتقل عنه والمنتقل إليه لا يعقل إلا إذا كانا موجودين. وههنا لما لم يكن المنتقل عنه ثابتا فلا ثبوت للانتقال أصلا، والموصوف لا ثبوت صفة له إلا إذا كان أصل الثبوت له، فإذا لا متوسط بين الوجود والعدم. (2) أقول: هذه الإشكالات لا تشكلت غير الأذهان التي تعودت الخمول ولم تألف النظر في الحقائق، والناظر المتميز لا يشك في أنها أغلاط ومغالطات.
And he has another exposition: that the quiddity (māhiyya), when it transitions from non-existence to existence, the act of transition necessitates that it be neither non-existent nor existent; because if it were non-existent, then it has not yet begun the transition but remains as it was before, and if it were existent, then the terminus to which it transitions has been attained, and at the moment of complete attainment of the terminus, the transition does not remain but ceases. So it is apparent that the occurrence of transition must be intermediate between that from which it transitions and that to which it transitions, so it is necessary that it be outside the limit of pure non-existence and not entering into the limit of pure existence. (1) So these are a drop from the oceans of difficulties that arise against our statement: 'A thing is either existent or non-existent.' And if the state of the strongest self-evident truths is like this, what do you think of the weaker? (2) The second argument against the deniers of self-evident truths: that we find the intellect judging many things with certainty by its judgment of primary truths, while such certainty is not permissible in them, and that necessitates suspicion upon the judgment of the intellect. Explanation of the first in several ways. ... His matter. In existence and non-existence and the relation between them, and nothing whose concept includes non-existence and relation to it exists in external reality. And temporality (ḥudūth) is a conceptual meaning that is an attribute obtained in the intellect when conceiving non-existence, and existence is ordered upon it in actuality, and the quiddity described by that attribute is not described by existence alone, so it is not existent in external reality but rather is existent in the intellect. And from that does not necessarily follow an intermediary between existence and non-existence; because the meaning of intermediary is that the quiddity in external reality be undescribed by existence and undescribed by non-existence, and that is impossible; because the quiddity's being in external reality is its external existence, so its being in external reality undescribed by existence is a contradiction. It cannot be said: the body at the instant of its transition from rest to motion is existent and not described by rest nor by motion, and it cannot be said that the body at that instant is existent only in the intellect, for then it would be at that time neither stationary nor moving, and an intermediary between the two opposites of rest and motion would follow. For we say: the existence of motion is only possible in time, and likewise the existence of rest, and their absence from a thing such that neither of them exists in it necessitates an intermediary between them. But the body at the instant which is the common limit between the time of rest and the time of motion is not such that motion or rest could exist in it. So from its existence at that instant does not follow the establishment of an intermediary between motion and rest by the body being described by both at that instant. And this is different from our case, because the quiddity at the instant in which it is not described by pure existence is not established; for its establishment is only in the state of its being described by existence. (1) I say: The beginning of transition and the cessation of transition are only valid if the transition occurs in something that exists gradually, like motion. As for when the transition is from nothing, there is no limit nor cessation, and the intermediary between that from which it transitions and that to which it transitions is only conceivable if both are existent. Here, since that from which it transitions is not established, there is no transition at all, and the described has no attribute established for it unless the foundation of establishment belongs to it. Therefore, there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence. (2) I say: These difficulties do not trouble other than minds that have become accustomed to obscurity and have not become familiar with examining realities, and the discerning observer does not doubt that they are errors and fallacies.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the law of excluded middle and then refutes it. It begins with an alternative exposition: if a quiddity transitions from non-existence to existence, the act of transition would require that it be neither non-existent nor existent at the moment of transition. This would contradict the principle that a thing is either existent or non-existent. The author then characterizes these as difficulties (ishkālāt) against the strongest self-evident truths. Next, he presents a second argument against those who deny self-evident truths: the intellect judges many things with certainty based on primary truths, yet if such judgments were open to doubt, suspicion would fall on all rational judgments. He then provides a detailed explanation of the first point. The explanation argues that the concept of an intermediary (wāsiṭa) between existence and non-existence is incoherent: a quiddity in external reality is either existent (which is its external existence) or not; to be in external reality without existence is contradictory. An objection regarding a body at the instant of transition from rest to motion is addressed: motion and rest require time, so at an instant (which is timeless), neither motion nor rest can occur, hence no intermediary exists. The author's own comment (أقول) reinforces that transition from nothing does not involve a real intermediate state, as the starting point (non-existence) is not established. In his final note, he dismisses the difficulties as errors that only trouble untrained minds.

Key Terms
ماهية (quiddity)
وجود (existence)
عدم (non-existence)
واسطة (intermediary)
انتقال (transition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the moment of coming into existence requires a real intermediate state.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that transition from non-existence is not a real process; it is only conceptual, so no actual intermediary exists.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's refutation, why is there no intermediate state during transition from non-existence to existence?
A) Because transition from nothing is not a real gradual process, and an intermediary requires both endpoints to exist.
B) Because the quiddity remains non-existent until the moment it becomes existent.
C) Because the intellect cannot conceive of a state that is neither existent nor non-existent.
D) Because the transition happens instantaneously, leaving no time for an intermediate state.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the idea of a middle state between existence and non-existence by showing that transition from nothing is not a real process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own moments of change—becoming a believer, or shifting in your convictions—do you tend to imagine a gradual middle ground? How does this logical clarity challenge you to see those transitions as instantaneous divine acts rather than processes you control?
?
If non-existence is not an entity, what does that mean for how you view your own past? Can you truly say you were 'nothing' before you were created, and does that knowledge change your gratitude for being brought into existence?
?
The law of excluded middle is a logical bedrock. Are you living as though moral and spiritual states are clear-cut, or do you sometimes hide in a comfortable 'middle' that this lesson declares to be unreal? Commit to one path today.

Defending Self-Evident Truths Against Skepticism
Imam Razi presents the second argument and the detailed refutation of the intermediary concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's second argument against deniers of self-evident truths and how the concept of an intermediary is fully refuted.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths are foundational; even objections do not undermine rational certainty.

Imam Razi presents a second argument: that the intellect judges many things with certainty based on primary truths, and if those judgments were open to doubt, all rational judgment would be suspect. He then provides a detailed explanation, using the example of motion and rest, and concludes that difficulties arise only from untrained minds.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
The argument states that the intellect's certainty in primary truths is strong, and any suspicion on them would taint all reasoning.
This argument is aimed at those who deny self-evident truths.
2
Study the detailed explanation of the first point
The explanation clarifies that the concept of an intermediary between existence and non-existence is incoherent, using the analogy of motion and rest.
The motion/rest analogy shows that an intermediary is not required, as at an instant, neither motion nor rest exists, but that does not imply an intermediary state.
3
Reflect on the final dismissal
Imam Razi dismisses the difficulties as errors that only trouble minds unaccustomed to examining realities.
This reinforces confidence in rational inquiry and self-evident truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله تقرير آخر وهو أن الماهية إذا انتقلت من العدم إلى الوجود فعال الانتقال لابد وأن تكون لا معدومة ولا موجودة؛ لأنها لو كانت معدومة فهي بعد لم تأخذ في الانتقال بل هي باقية كما كانت قبل ذلك، ولو كانت موجودة فقد حصل المنتقل إليها، وحين حصول المنتقل إليها بتمامه لم يبق الانتقال بل ينقطع، فظاهر أن حصول الانتقال لابد وأن يكون متوسطا بين المنتقل عنه والمنتقل إليه، فوجب أن يكون خارجا عن حد العدم الصرف وغير داخل إلى حد الوجود الصرف (1)

فهذه الإشكالات قطرة من بحار الإشكالات الواردة على قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون وأما أن لا يكون، وإذا كان حال أقوى البديهيات كذلك فما ظنك بالأضعف (2)

الحجة الثانية: لمنكري البديهيات: أنا نجد العقل جازما بأمور كثيرة بجزمه بالأوليات مع أن الجزم غير جائز فيها، وذلك يوجب تطرق التهمة إلى حكم العقل.

بيان الأول من وجوه.
..
شأنه
ب الوجود والعدم ونسبة بينهما ولا شيء مما يدخل في مفهومه العدم والنسبة إليه موجودة في الخارج، والحدوث معنى معقول هو صفة تحصل في العقل عند تعقل العدم، والوجود المرتب عليه في الفعل والماهية الموصوفة بتلك الصفة لا تكون موصوفة بالوجود وحده فلا تكون موجودة في الخارج بل إنما تكون موجودة في العقل، ولا يلزم من ذلك واسطة بين الوجود والعدم؛ لأن معنى الواسطة أن تكون الماهية في الخارج غير موصوفة بالوجود ولا بالعدم وذلك محال؛ لأن كون الماهية في الخارج هو وجودها الخارجي فكونها في الخارج غير موصوفة بالوجود تناقض. لا يقال الجسم في آن انتقاله من المكون إلى الحركة موجود وليس بموصوف بالسكون ولا بالحركة ولا يمكن أن يقال: الجسم في ذلك الآن موجود في العقل فقط فإذا هو في ذلك الزمان لا ساكن ولا متحرك ويلزم منه واسطة بين السكون والحركة المتقابلين. لانا نقول: وجود الحركة لا يمكن إلا في زمان، وكذلك وجود السكون، وانتفاؤهما عن شيء من أن يوجد أحدهما فيه يقتضي واسطة بينهما، لكن الجسم في الآن الذي هو الفعل المشترك بين زمان المسكون وزمان الحركة لا يكون من شأنه أن يوجد فيه حركة أو سكون، فلا يلزم من وجوده في ذلك الآن ثبوت واسطة بين الحركة والسكون بكون الجسم موصوفا بهما في ذلك الآن، وهذا بخلاف ما نحن فيه، لأن الماهية في الآن الذي لا تكون موصوفة بالوجود المحض لا يكون ثابتة، فإن ثبوتها في حال اتصافها بالوجود فقط. (1) أقول: الأخذ في الانتقال وانقطاع الانتقال لا يصحان إلا إذا كان الانتقال واقعا في شيء موجود بالتدريج كالحركة، أما إذا كان الانتقال من لا شيء فلا يكون هناك حد ولا انقطاع، والمتوسط بين المنتقل عنه والمنتقل إليه لا يعقل إلا إذا كانا موجودين. وههنا لما لم يكن المنتقل عنه ثابتا فلا ثبوت للانتقال أصلا، والموصوف لا ثبوت صفة له إلا إذا كان أصل الثبوت له، فإذا لا متوسط بين الوجود والعدم. (2) أقول: هذه الإشكالات لا تشكلت غير الأذهان التي تعودت الخمول ولم تألف النظر في الحقائق، والناظر المتميز لا يشك في أنها أغلاط ومغالطات.
And he has another exposition: that the quiddity (māhiyya), when it transitions from non-existence to existence, the act of transition necessitates that it be neither non-existent nor existent; because if it were non-existent, then it has not yet begun the transition but remains as it was before, and if it were existent, then the terminus to which it transitions has been attained, and at the moment of complete attainment of the terminus, the transition does not remain but ceases. So it is apparent that the occurrence of transition must be intermediate between that from which it transitions and that to which it transitions, so it is necessary that it be outside the limit of pure non-existence and not entering into the limit of pure existence. (1) So these are a drop from the oceans of difficulties that arise against our statement: 'A thing is either existent or non-existent.' And if the state of the strongest self-evident truths is like this, what do you think of the weaker? (2) The second argument against the deniers of self-evident truths: that we find the intellect judging many things with certainty by its judgment of primary truths, while such certainty is not permissible in them, and that necessitates suspicion upon the judgment of the intellect. Explanation of the first in several ways. ... His matter. In existence and non-existence and the relation between them, and nothing whose concept includes non-existence and relation to it exists in external reality. And temporality (ḥudūth) is a conceptual meaning that is an attribute obtained in the intellect when conceiving non-existence, and existence is ordered upon it in actuality, and the quiddity described by that attribute is not described by existence alone, so it is not existent in external reality but rather is existent in the intellect. And from that does not necessarily follow an intermediary between existence and non-existence; because the meaning of intermediary is that the quiddity in external reality be undescribed by existence and undescribed by non-existence, and that is impossible; because the quiddity's being in external reality is its external existence, so its being in external reality undescribed by existence is a contradiction. It cannot be said: the body at the instant of its transition from rest to motion is existent and not described by rest nor by motion, and it cannot be said that the body at that instant is existent only in the intellect, for then it would be at that time neither stationary nor moving, and an intermediary between the two opposites of rest and motion would follow. For we say: the existence of motion is only possible in time, and likewise the existence of rest, and their absence from a thing such that neither of them exists in it necessitates an intermediary between them. But the body at the instant which is the common limit between the time of rest and the time of motion is not such that motion or rest could exist in it. So from its existence at that instant does not follow the establishment of an intermediary between motion and rest by the body being described by both at that instant. And this is different from our case, because the quiddity at the instant in which it is not described by pure existence is not established; for its establishment is only in the state of its being described by existence. (1) I say: The beginning of transition and the cessation of transition are only valid if the transition occurs in something that exists gradually, like motion. As for when the transition is from nothing, there is no limit nor cessation, and the intermediary between that from which it transitions and that to which it transitions is only conceivable if both are existent. Here, since that from which it transitions is not established, there is no transition at all, and the described has no attribute established for it unless the foundation of establishment belongs to it. Therefore, there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence. (2) I say: These difficulties do not trouble other than minds that have become accustomed to obscurity and have not become familiar with examining realities, and the discerning observer does not doubt that they are errors and fallacies.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the law of excluded middle and then refutes it. It begins with an alternative exposition: if a quiddity transitions from non-existence to existence, the act of transition would require that it be neither non-existent nor existent at the moment of transition. This would contradict the principle that a thing is either existent or non-existent. The author then characterizes these as difficulties (ishkālāt) against the strongest self-evident truths. Next, he presents a second argument against those who deny self-evident truths: the intellect judges many things with certainty based on primary truths, yet if such judgments were open to doubt, suspicion would fall on all rational judgments. He then provides a detailed explanation of the first point. The explanation argues that the concept of an intermediary (wāsiṭa) between existence and non-existence is incoherent: a quiddity in external reality is either existent (which is its external existence) or not; to be in external reality without existence is contradictory. An objection regarding a body at the instant of transition from rest to motion is addressed: motion and rest require time, so at an instant (which is timeless), neither motion nor rest can occur, hence no intermediary exists. The author's own comment (أقول) reinforces that transition from nothing does not involve a real intermediate state, as the starting point (non-existence) is not established. In his final note, he dismisses the difficulties as errors that only trouble untrained minds.

Key Terms
بديهيات (self-evident truths)
عقل (intellect)
جزم (certainty)
واسطة (intermediary)
حركة (motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the instant of transition from rest to motion implies an intermediary state between rest and motion.
Correction: Imam Razi explains that motion and rest require time, so at an instant, neither occurs, and no intermediary state is implied. Similarly, between existence and non-existence, no intermediary exists.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why does the body at the instant of transition from rest to motion not imply an intermediary between rest and motion?
A) Because motion and rest require time, and at an instant neither can occur.
B) Because the body is existent only in the intellect at that instant.
C) Because the body is both at rest and in motion simultaneously.
D) Because the instant is indivisible and thus cannot be considered.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi defends self-evident truths by showing that objections to the law of excluded middle are based on misunderstandings and do not undermine rational certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone presents a sophisticated objection against a belief you hold, do you immediately doubt your certainty, or do you examine the argument carefully? How can you cultivate the 'discernment' Razi describes without becoming arrogant in your convictions?
?
Razi dismisses certain objections as errors that only trouble untrained minds. Where in your own intellectual life have you accepted confusion instead of insisting on clarity? What fundamental truth are you willing to defend even when it's challenged?
?
This lesson challenges you to hold fast to the law of excluded middle. Is there an area of your life where you are trying to maintain a middle ground between truth and falsehood? What step will you take today to embrace the clarity of opposing alternatives?

The Skeptical Objection from Perceptual Continuity
Imam Razi presents a skeptical objection against the certainty of perceptual identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptical objection that perceptual identity judgments cannot be certain because of remote metaphysical possibilities.

Core Idea
Even obvious perceptual certainty can be doubted if any metaphysical possibility is allowed.

Imam Razi presents a powerful skeptical challenge: when we see Zayd, close our eyes, and see him again, how can we be certain it is the same person? The objector argues that God could have annihilated the first Zayd and created an exact copy (on Muslim grounds) or that a strange form in the hyle could produce a separate but identical individual (on philosophical grounds). This lesson presents the objection as it appears in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two versions: one based on Muslim theology, one on philosophy.
The objection is meant to challenge the certainty of self-evident truths.
2
Identify the core doubt
What is the exact possibility that the objector raises? Why does it make the judgment 'not permissible'?
3
Reflect on the implications
If this doubt were valid, what would it mean for our everyday knowledge? This sets up the refutation in subsequent lessons.
Consider how this relates to the concept of 'self-evident truths' (badihiyyat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أنا إذا رأينا زيدا ثم غمضنا العين لحظة ثم فتحنا في الحال وشاهدنا زيدا مرة أخرى جزمنا أن زيدا الذي شاهدناه ثانيا هو الذي شاهدناه أولا، وهذا الجزم غير جائز لاحتمال أن الله تعالى أعدم الزيد الأول في تلك اللحظة التي غمضنا العين فيها وخلق في الحال مثله وهذا على مذهب المسلمين، وأما على مذهب الفلاسفة فله شكل غريب فلكي اقتضى هذا النوع في التصرف في هيولى عالم الكون والإنسان، وهو إن كان بعيدا جدا لكنه جائز عندهم، وعلى هذا التقدير يكون الزيد الذي شاهدناه ثانيا غير الزيد الأول (1)

(1) أقول: العقل جازم بلا تردد أن هذا الزيد هو الأول، ولو كان حكمه موقوفا على نفي الاحتمال المذكور نظريا لا بديهيا. والمسلمون لم يتفقوا على أن إعدام الموجود إنما يمكن. قالوا المؤثر هو كل موجود يحصل من موجود هو هو أثر. ولهذا ذهب المعتزلة إلى أن الإعدام يكون بإيجاد ضد الوجود حتى مشايخهم قالوا: إن الله تعالى قبل القيامة يخلق عرضا هو الفناء لا في محل وهو ضد جميع ما سوى الله تعالى، فيفنى بوجوده ما سوى الله تعالى، وهو لا يبقى زمانين فينتفي ولا شيء غير وجه الله تعالى. وذهب النظام إلى أن جميع الأجسام والأعراض غير باقية زمانين بل يحدثه الله تعالى حالا فحالا. وذهبت الأشاعرة إلى مثل هذا القول في الأعراض. وقال جميع من لا يجوز إعادة المعدوم: بأن الأجسام لا تفنى ولكن تفنى التأليفات التي بين أجزائها فيكون لأجل ذلك هالكة، فإعدام زيد الأول ليس بمتفق عليه عند أكثر المسلمين، وما لا يتفق عليه لا يكون مقدورا للفاعل المختار. وأما على مذهب الفلاسفة: فالشكل الغريب لا يكون سببيا فاعليا ولا بد معه من سبب قابل حتى يحصل الأثر، ومادة زيد الأول ونفسه لا يمكن أن تفنى، ومادة زيد الثاني لا يمكن أن تتصل بها صورته إلا بعد حصول اعتدال إنساني وتغذية ونشو حتى يصير بعد مدة من الزمان إنسانا كاملا. فهذه الدعوى على المسلمين وعلى الفلاسفة غير مطابقة لمذاهبهم، وهب أنهم يقولون بذلك إلا أن العقل لما كان جازما بنفي ذلك الاحتمال لا يقع للعقلاء شك في البديهيات بسبب أمثال هذه الخرافات.
One of them: when we see Zayd, then close the eye for a moment, then open immediately and see Zayd again, we judge with certainty that the Zayd we saw second is the one we saw first. But this certainty is not permissible due to the possibility that God, exalted, annihilated the first Zayd in that moment when we closed the eye and created the like of him immediately, and this is according to the doctrine of the Muslims. As for the doctrine of the philosophers, there is a strange form for him, because this type of operation in the hyle of the world of generation and man is required, and although it is very remote, it is possible according to them. On this assumption, the Zayd we saw second is other than the first Zayd. (1) (1) I say: The intellect judges with certainty without hesitation that this Zayd is the first, even if its judgment were dependent on negating the mentioned possibility theoretically, not intuitively. The Muslims have not agreed that the annihilation of an existing thing is possible. They said: the efficient cause is every existent from which an existent that is the very same effect arises. For this reason, the Mu'tazila held that annihilation occurs by bringing into existence the opposite of existence; even their elders said: God, exalted, before the Resurrection creates an accident which is annihilation (fanā') not in a substrate, and it is the opposite of everything other than God, so by its existence everything other than God perishes, and it does not remain for two moments, so it ceases and nothing remains except the face of God. Al-Naẓẓām held that all bodies and accidents do not remain for two moments, but God creates them moment by moment. The Ash'aris held a similar view concerning accidents. And all those who do not permit the return of the non-existent said that bodies do not perish, but the compositions between their parts perish, and because of that they become destroyed. So the annihilation of the first Zayd is not agreed upon by most Muslims, and what is not agreed upon cannot be within the power of the free agent. As for the doctrine of the philosophers: the strange form is not an efficient cause, and there must be with it a receptive cause for the effect to occur, and the matter of the first Zayd and his soul cannot perish, and the matter of the second Zayd cannot be united with his form except after the attainment of human moderation, nutrition, and growth until after a period of time he becomes a perfect human. So this claim, with respect to Muslims and philosophers, does not conform to their doctrines. Even if they did say that, the intellect, since it is certain in negating that possibility, no suspicion occurs to rational people concerning self-evident truths due to such absurdities.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents one of the skeptical objections against self-evident truths: the case of seeing a known person (Zayd) twice with eyes closed in between. The skeptic argues that we cannot be certain it is the same Zayd because God could have annihilated the first and created an exact copy in that instant, or on the philosophers' view, a strange form in the hyle could produce a distinct but identical individual. The author's refutation proceeds in stages: first, the intellect's certainty is intuitive and not dependent on negating remote possibilities; second, Muslim and philosopher doctrines do not actually allow such annihilation or replacement as described. He details various Muslim schools: the Mu'tazila on annihilation by creating an opposite, al-Naẓẓām's moment-by-moment creation, Ash'ari view on accidents, and the position that bodies persist but compositions perish—showing lack of consensus on the possibility of annihilating Zayd. For philosophers, the 'strange form' is not an efficient cause and requires a receptive cause; moreover, the matter and soul of Zayd cannot perish, and a new form cannot attach instantly. Hence, the claimed scenario is not even possible in their own frameworks. The passage concludes that such far-fetched possibilities do not undermine rational certainty in self-evident truths.

Key Terms
جزم (certainty)
إعدام (annihilation)
هيولى (hyle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objection is a valid refutation of self-evident truths and cannot be answered.
Correction: The objection only shows that remote possibilities can be conceived, but the refutation will demonstrate that these possibilities are not actually consistent with the doctrines they invoke, and that the intellect's certainty is intuitive.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the objector's claim that we cannot be certain the second Zayd is the same as the first?
A) Our memory is unreliable.
B) A remote possibility exists that God annihilated the first and created a copy, or that philosophy allows an identical form to arise.
C) Zayd might have changed during the blink.
D) The philosophers deny the existence of matter.

Key Takeaway
The objection attempts to undermine self-evident truths by invoking remote metaphysical possibilities that are not actually consistent with the doctrines they claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at someone you love, do you ever consider the possibility that God could replace them in an instant? How does this thought affect your trust in your own perceptions and your relationship with them?
?
This passage suggests that even our most basic certainties can be shaken by mere possibility. Are there convictions in your life that you hold with absolute certainty? Would you still hold them if you allowed yourself to entertain such possibilities?
?
The objection relies on God's power to annihilate and recreate. How do you reconcile the idea of a trustworthy God who preserves His creation with the logical possibility that He might not? Does this challenge your confidence in divine consistency?

Refutation: Intuitive Certainty and Muslim Doctrines
Imam Razi refutes the objection by asserting intuitive certainty and analyzing Muslim doctrines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's first line of refutation: the intellect's certainty is intuitive, and the Muslim schools do not actually allow the annihilation scenario.

Core Idea
The intellect judges intuitively, and Muslim doctrinal diversity precludes the objector's assumption.

Imam Razi begins his refutation by asserting that the intellect's certainty in this case is intuitive, not dependent on theoretical negation. He then examines the Muslim theological schools, showing that there is no consensus on the possibility of annihilating a person like Zayd. Each school's specific views actually rule out the scenario the objector relies on.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the assertion of intuitive certainty at the start.
2
Study the Muslim schools' views
Identify the Mu'tazila, al-Naẓẓām, Ash'aris, and the non-return of the non-existent view. Note how each either denies annihilation or requires a specific process.
Understanding these positions is key to seeing why the objection fails.
3
Connect to the refutation
How does the lack of consensus and the specific doctrines undermine the objector's claim?
The objector assumed that annihilation was generally accepted, but it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أنا إذا رأينا زيدا ثم غمضنا العين لحظة ثم فتحنا في الحال وشاهدنا زيدا مرة أخرى جزمنا أن زيدا الذي شاهدناه ثانيا هو الذي شاهدناه أولا، وهذا الجزم غير جائز لاحتمال أن الله تعالى أعدم الزيد الأول في تلك اللحظة التي غمضنا العين فيها وخلق في الحال مثله وهذا على مذهب المسلمين، وأما على مذهب الفلاسفة فله شكل غريب فلكي اقتضى هذا النوع في التصرف في هيولى عالم الكون والإنسان، وهو إن كان بعيدا جدا لكنه جائز عندهم، وعلى هذا التقدير يكون الزيد الذي شاهدناه ثانيا غير الزيد الأول (1)

(1) أقول: العقل جازم بلا تردد أن هذا الزيد هو الأول، ولو كان حكمه موقوفا على نفي الاحتمال المذكور نظريا لا بديهيا. والمسلمون لم يتفقوا على أن إعدام الموجود إنما يمكن. قالوا المؤثر هو كل موجود يحصل من موجود هو هو أثر. ولهذا ذهب المعتزلة إلى أن الإعدام يكون بإيجاد ضد الوجود حتى مشايخهم قالوا: إن الله تعالى قبل القيامة يخلق عرضا هو الفناء لا في محل وهو ضد جميع ما سوى الله تعالى، فيفنى بوجوده ما سوى الله تعالى، وهو لا يبقى زمانين فينتفي ولا شيء غير وجه الله تعالى. وذهب النظام إلى أن جميع الأجسام والأعراض غير باقية زمانين بل يحدثه الله تعالى حالا فحالا. وذهبت الأشاعرة إلى مثل هذا القول في الأعراض. وقال جميع من لا يجوز إعادة المعدوم: بأن الأجسام لا تفنى ولكن تفنى التأليفات التي بين أجزائها فيكون لأجل ذلك هالكة، فإعدام زيد الأول ليس بمتفق عليه عند أكثر المسلمين، وما لا يتفق عليه لا يكون مقدورا للفاعل المختار. وأما على مذهب الفلاسفة: فالشكل الغريب لا يكون سببيا فاعليا ولا بد معه من سبب قابل حتى يحصل الأثر، ومادة زيد الأول ونفسه لا يمكن أن تفنى، ومادة زيد الثاني لا يمكن أن تتصل بها صورته إلا بعد حصول اعتدال إنساني وتغذية ونشو حتى يصير بعد مدة من الزمان إنسانا كاملا. فهذه الدعوى على المسلمين وعلى الفلاسفة غير مطابقة لمذاهبهم، وهب أنهم يقولون بذلك إلا أن العقل لما كان جازما بنفي ذلك الاحتمال لا يقع للعقلاء شك في البديهيات بسبب أمثال هذه الخرافات.
One of them: when we see Zayd, then close the eye for a moment, then open immediately and see Zayd again, we judge with certainty that the Zayd we saw second is the one we saw first. But this certainty is not permissible due to the possibility that God, exalted, annihilated the first Zayd in that moment when we closed the eye and created the like of him immediately, and this is according to the doctrine of the Muslims. As for the doctrine of the philosophers, there is a strange form for him, because this type of operation in the hyle of the world of generation and man is required, and although it is very remote, it is possible according to them. On this assumption, the Zayd we saw second is other than the first Zayd. (1) (1) I say: The intellect judges with certainty without hesitation that this Zayd is the first, even if its judgment were dependent on negating the mentioned possibility theoretically, not intuitively. The Muslims have not agreed that the annihilation of an existing thing is possible. They said: the efficient cause is every existent from which an existent that is the very same effect arises. For this reason, the Mu'tazila held that annihilation occurs by bringing into existence the opposite of existence; even their elders said: God, exalted, before the Resurrection creates an accident which is annihilation (fanā') not in a substrate, and it is the opposite of everything other than God, so by its existence everything other than God perishes, and it does not remain for two moments, so it ceases and nothing remains except the face of God. Al-Naẓẓām held that all bodies and accidents do not remain for two moments, but God creates them moment by moment. The Ash'aris held a similar view concerning accidents. And all those who do not permit the return of the non-existent said that bodies do not perish, but the compositions between their parts perish, and because of that they become destroyed. So the annihilation of the first Zayd is not agreed upon by most Muslims, and what is not agreed upon cannot be within the power of the free agent. As for the doctrine of the philosophers: the strange form is not an efficient cause, and there must be with it a receptive cause for the effect to occur, and the matter of the first Zayd and his soul cannot perish, and the matter of the second Zayd cannot be united with his form except after the attainment of human moderation, nutrition, and growth until after a period of time he becomes a perfect human. So this claim, with respect to Muslims and philosophers, does not conform to their doctrines. Even if they did say that, the intellect, since it is certain in negating that possibility, no suspicion occurs to rational people concerning self-evident truths due to such absurdities.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents one of the skeptical objections against self-evident truths: the case of seeing a known person (Zayd) twice with eyes closed in between. The skeptic argues that we cannot be certain it is the same Zayd because God could have annihilated the first and created an exact copy in that instant, or on the philosophers' view, a strange form in the hyle could produce a distinct but identical individual. The author's refutation proceeds in stages: first, the intellect's certainty is intuitive and not dependent on negating remote possibilities; second, Muslim and philosopher doctrines do not actually allow such annihilation or replacement as described. He details various Muslim schools: the Mu'tazila on annihilation by creating an opposite, al-Naẓẓām's moment-by-moment creation, Ash'ari view on accidents, and the position that bodies persist but compositions perish—showing lack of consensus on the possibility of annihilating Zayd. For philosophers, the 'strange form' is not an efficient cause and requires a receptive cause; moreover, the matter and soul of Zayd cannot perish, and a new form cannot attach instantly. Hence, the claimed scenario is not even possible in their own frameworks. The passage concludes that such far-fetched possibilities do not undermine rational certainty in self-evident truths.

Key Terms
بديهيات (self-evident truths)
إعدام (annihilation)
فناء (fanā')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Muslims generally accept the possibility of instantaneous annihilation and replacement of a person.
Correction: The passage shows that there is no consensus; each school's doctrine either denies or complicates such a scenario.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what is the Mu'tazila's explanation of annihilation?
A) It is a direct act of God annihilating the substance.
B) It occurs by creating an opposite of existence (fanā') that destroys everything except God.
C) It is the same as the Ash'ari view on accidents.
D) It is impossible because bodies are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The objector's scenario is not actually supported by Muslim doctrines; the intellect's certainty is intuitive and unaffected by remote and doctrinally inconsistent possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are certain of something in your heart—like the oneness of God or the truth of Islam—do you let remote, hypothetical doubts shake that certainty? How does Razi's claim that the intellect judges intuitively challenge you to stop entertaining every 'what if' that has no real doctrinal support?
?
The objector's scenario assumed a premise not universally agreed upon by Muslims. Think of a doubt you have encountered about your faith. Is it based on a real, agreed-upon doctrine, or on a hypothetical scenario that has no basis in Islamic teachings? What would it take for you to return to the intuitive certainty of your heart?
?
Imam Razi shows that the intellect does not need to eliminate every possibility to be certain. Where in your life have you allowed the fear of a remote possibility to paralyze your conviction? What action can you take today to align your certainty with the clear judgment of your intellect?

Refutation: Philosophical Doctrines and Final Conclusion
Imam Razi refutes the philosophical version and concludes the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the philosophical version of the objection and concludes that such absurdities do not affect self-evident truths.

Core Idea
Philosophical hyle theory cannot produce instant identical humans, and the intellect’s certainty overrides remote fancies.

Imam Razi now addresses the philosophers' version of the objection. He argues that the 'strange form' is not an efficient cause and requires a receptive cause; the matter and soul of Zayd cannot perish, and a new form cannot attach instantly. He then delivers the final conclusion: such far-fetched possibilities do not cause rational people to doubt self-evident truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophical refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. Understand the argument about the form not being an efficient cause.
2
Analyze the causal argument
Why can't the strange form act alone? What is needed for a new human to form?
Note the conditions: receptive cause, matter of first Zayd persists, and growth takes time.
3
Understand the final conclusion
Razi ends by stating that such absurdities do not create doubt in self-evident truths for rational people.
This ties back to the intuitive certainty asserted earlier.

Refuting the Skeptical Objection from Developmental Continuity
Imam Razi refutes the skeptical objection about developmental continuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the skeptical objection about gradual human development by appealing to degrees of certainty.

Core Idea
The intellect does not doubt its judgment even if that certainty is weaker than primary truths.

This lesson examines a skeptical objection that challenges our certainty about human development from childhood to old age. Imam Razi responds by acknowledging that while this certainty is not as strong as primary self-evident truths, it is sufficient to reject the doubt. The passage illustrates the use of degrees of certainty in theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection claims that our necessary knowledge of gradual development could be false because God could create an adult instantly, or the philosophers' 'strange form' could produce an adult. Understand the two frameworks presented.
The objection uses hypothetical possibilities to undermine certainty.
2
Examine the beginning of the response
Imam Razi states that the intellect does not doubt its judgment in this matter, even if this certainty is not like the certainty of primary truths. This shows he accepts degrees of certainty.
The response is cut off, but the key point is that the doubt is dismissed despite imperfect certainty.
3
Reflect on degrees of certainty
Consider why the possibility of an instant creation does not negate the practical certainty we have about gradual development. How does this protect the argument?
This illustrates that theological arguments often rely on sufficient, not absolute, certainty.

الثاني: أنا إنا شاهدنا إنسانا شابا أو شيخا علمنا بالضرورة أنه ما خلق الآن دفعة واحدة من غير أب وأم بل كان قبل ذلك طفلا ومترعرعا وشابا حتى صار الآن شيخا، وهذا الجزم غير ثابت، أما على مذهب المسلمين فالفاعل المختار، وأما على مذهب الفلاسفة فالشكل الغريب. (2) أقول: العقل لا يشك فيما جزم بسبب هذا القول الذي قاله وإن لم يكن هذا الجزم مثل الجزم بأن
The second: we have seen a young man or an old man, and we know necessarily that he was not created now all at once without father and mother, but rather before that he was a child, a youth, and a young man until he became now an old man. But this certainty is not established—according to the doctrine of the Muslims, because the free agent (could create him instantly), and according to the doctrine of the philosophers, because of the strange form. Commentary
This passage presents the second skeptical objection: our necessary knowledge that a person develops gradually from childhood to old age is not certain, because God (on Muslim view) could create that person instantly as an old man, or the philosophers' strange form could produce the same. The author's response (cut off at page end) begins by affirming that the intellect does not doubt what it judges with certainty in this regard, although this certainty is not of the same level as that in primary self-evident truths. The response suggests that even if the certainty is weaker, it is sufficient to reject the skeptical doubt. The incomplete sentence implies a comparison to stronger certainties, but the argument is that the natural human certainty about development is not undermined by such remote possibilities.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all certainties must be equally strong, or that any possibility of error nullifies knowledge.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that even if a certainty is weaker than primary truths, it can still be sufficient for knowledge and for rejecting skeptical doubts.

How does Imam Razi respond to the objection that we cannot be certain of gradual human development because God could create an old man instantly?
A) He says the intellect does not doubt its judgment in this matter, even if it is not as strong as primary certainties.
B) He says that such a scenario is impossible because God does not deceive.
C) He says that the philosophers' view prevents any doubt.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the skeptical objection by affirming that the intellect's certainty about gradual development, though not absolute, is sufficient to dismiss the doubt.

?

?

The First Doubt: Annihilation and Replacement Does Not Undermine Certainty
Razi presents and refutes the first doubt about identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the possibility of God annihilating Zayd and creating a similar one does not invalidate our self-evident certainty about identity.

Core Idea
Remote possibilities do not shake necessary knowledge.

Imam Razi confronts a philosophical doubt that attempts to undermine our most basic certainty: that we remain the same person over time. The objector suggests that for all we know, God could have annihilated the first Zayd and created a copy in the blink of an eye. Razi responds by first asserting the intellect's unhesitating certainty, then showing that the proposed scenario is not even admitted by the schools of thought the objector claims to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and Razi's initial response
Read the Arabic passage that presents the doubt: 'When we see Zayd, close our eyes, then open and see him again, we are certain he is the same. But this certainty is not valid because God could have annihilated the first and created a like.' Then read Razi's immediate counter: 'The intellect is certain without hesitation.'
Notice that Razi does not begin by analyzing the possibility; he appeals directly to the intellect's certainty.
2
Examine the Muslim schools' positions on annihilation
Razi lists several Muslim views: Mu'tazila, al-Nazzam, Ash'arites, and those who disallow recreation of the non-existent. Identify which view supports the objector's claim, if any.
Most Muslims do not accept the annihilation of a permanent being like Zayd's matter.
3
Analyze the philosophers' position
Razi explains that on the philosophers' view, the 'strange form' is not an efficient cause by itself; it requires a receptive cause and time. Thus, the instantaneous replacement of Zayd is not possible.
The matter and soul of Zayd are imperishable in their system.
4
Draw the conclusion
Conclude that the objector's possibility is not actual in any school, and even if it were, the intellect's certainty in self-evident truths is not shaken by remote absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها : أنا إذا رأينا زيدا ثم غمضنا العين لحظة ثم فتحنا فى الحال وشاهدنا زيدا مرة أخرى جزمنا أن زيدا الذي شاهدناه ثانيا هو الذي شاهدناه أولا ، وهذا الجزم غير جائز لاحتمال أن الله تعالى أعدم الزيد الأول في تلك اللحظة التي غمضنا العين فيها وخلق فى الحال مثله وهذا على مذهب المسلمين ، وأما على مذهب الفلاسفة فلم له حدث شكل غريب فل كى اقتضى هذا النوع في التصرف في هيولى عالم الكون و الانسان ، وهو إن كان بعيدا جدا لكنه جائز عندهم ، وعلى هذا التقدير يكون الزيد الذي شاهدناه ثانيا غير الزيد الأول (١)

(1) أقول : العقل جازم بلا تردد أن هذا الزيد هو الأول ولو كان حكمه موقوفا على نفى الاحتمال المذكور نظريا لابديهيا ، والمسلمون لم يتفقوا على أن إعدام الموجودات بممكن. قالوا المؤثر هو كل موجود يحصل من موجود هو هو أثر . ولهذا ذهب المعتزلة إلى أن الإعدام يكون بإيجاد ضد الوجود حتى مشايخهم قالوا : إن الله تعالى قبل القيامة يخلق عرضا هو الفناء لا فى محل وهو ضد جميع ما سوى الله تعالى ، فيفنى بوجوده ما سوى الله تعالى وهو لا يبقى زمانين فينتفى ولا شيء غير وجه الله تعالى . وذهب النظام إلى أن جميع الأجسام والأعراض غير باقية زمانين بل يحدثه الله تعالى حالا فحالا . وذهبت الأشاعرة إلى مثل هذا القول في الأعراض. وقال جميع من لا يجوز إعادة المعدوم : بأن الأجسام لا تفنى ولكن تفنى التأليفات التي بين أجزائها فيكون لأجل ذلك هالكة ، فإعدام زيد الأول ليس بممكن عند أكثر المسلمين ، وما لا يمكن لا يكون مقدوراً للفاعل المختار . وأما على مذهب الفلاسفة : فالشكل الغريب لا يكون لازما فاعليا ولابد معه من سبب قابل دين يحصل الأثر ، ومادة زيد الأول ونفسه لا يمكن أن تفنى ، ومادة زيد الثانى لا يمكن أن تتصل بها صورته إلا بعد حصول اعتدال إنسانى وتغذية ونشو حتى يصير بعد مرور مدة من الزمان إنسانا كاملا ، فهذه الدعوى على المسلمين وعلى الفلاسفة غير مطابقة لمذاهبهم ، وهب أنهم يقولون بذلك إلا أن العقل لما كان جازما بنفى ذلك الاحتمال لا يقع للعقلاء شك فى البديهيات بسبب أمثال هذه الخرافات

فإن قيل : وكيف حال معجزات الأنبياء عليهم السلام ؟

قلت : ليس في معجزاتهم إعدام شيء باق ، فإن جعل العصا حية ثم إعادتها إلى سيرتها الأولى ليس إلا تبديل صورة بصورة، وإخراج الناقة من الجبل وانفجار الماء من الجبل وانفجار الماء من الحجر وإحياء الموتى وغير ذلك أمور ممكنة في العقل ليس فيها إعدام باق ، وإيجاد مثل للمتقدم دفعة مع أن لبعضها تأويلات عليه لا يمكن إيرادها هنا .
One of them: When we see Zayd, then close the eye for a moment, then open immediately and see Zayd again, we are certain that Zayd whom we saw second is the one we saw first. But this certainty is not valid because of the possibility that Allah the Exalted annihilated the first Zayd in that moment when we closed the eye and created his like immediately, and this is according to the school of the Muslims. As for the school of the philosophers, there occurred for them a strange form [؟] such that this kind of action was required in the hyle (hayula) of the world of generation and man, and although it is extremely remote, it is possible for them. On this assumption, the Zayd we saw second is not the first Zayd. (1) I say: The intellect is certain without hesitation that this Zayd is the first, even if its judgment were dependent on denying the mentioned possibility theoretically and of primary necessity (badihiyya). The Muslims have not agreed that the annihilation of existing things is possible. They say: The effective agent (al-mu'aththir) is every existing thing that brings about from an existent an effect that is the same [ً؟]. For this reason, the Mu'tazila held that annihilation occurs by bringing into existence a contrary of existence, so much so that their leaders said: Allah before the Resurrection will create an accident which is annihilation (al-fana') not in a locus, and it is the contrary of everything other than Allah, so by its existence everything other than Allah is annihilated, and it does not remain for two moments, so it ceases and nothing remains except the face of Allah. And al-Nazzam held that all bodies and accidents do not remain for two moments, but Allah brings them into existence state after state. And the Ash'arites held a similar opinion regarding accidents. And all those who do not permit the recreation of the non-existent said: Bodies do not perish, but the compositions (al-ta'lifat) between their parts perish, and due to that they become ruined. So the annihilation of the first Zayd is not possible according to most Muslims, and what is not possible is not within the power of the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar). As for the school of the philosophers: The strange form is not an efficient cause [؟], and a receptive cause is necessary with it so that the effect occurs, and the matter (madda) of the first Zayd and his soul cannot perish, and the matter of the second Zayd cannot be connected to his form except after the occurrence of a human temperament (i'tidal insani) and nourishment and growth until he becomes, after the passage of a period of time, a perfect human. So this claim, both for the Muslims and for the philosophers, does not correspond to their doctrines. Even if we grant that they say that, since the intellect is certain of denying that possibility, no intelligent person falls into doubt about self-evident truths (badihiyyat) due to such absurdities (khurafat). If it is said: What about the miracles (mu'jizat) of the prophets, peace be upon them? I say: There is no annihilation of a permanent thing in their miracles. For making the stick a serpent and then returning it to its former state is only a change of form to form, and the bringing out of the she-camel from the mountain, and the gushing of water from the mountain and the gushing of water from the rock, and the reviving of the dead and other things are things possible in the intellect; there is no annihilation of a permanent thing in them, nor the bringing into existence of a likeness of a predecessor instantaneously, though some of them have interpretations that cannot be presented here.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first doubt (shubha) aimed at undermining self-evident knowledge (badihiyyat). The objector argues that our certainty that the same Zayd is seen after blinking is not absolute, because God could have annihilated the first and created a similar one (on Muslim premises) or because a strange 'form' in the philosophers' system could cause a similar effect. Razi responds with a two-part answer. First, he asserts that the intellect is unhesitatingly certain of identity, and this certainty is not dependent on theoretical refutation of the possibility. He then demonstrates that the supposed possibility is not admitted by either Muslims or philosophers. Among Muslims, the schools differ: the Mu'tazila maintain annihilation occurs by creating a contrary (al-fana'), al-Nazzam and the Ash'arites hold that bodies and accidents are re-created moment by moment, and others hold that compositions perish but not the bodies themselves. So annihilation of a permanent thing is not possible for most Muslims, and what is impossible cannot be attributed to the free agent. For philosophers, the 'strange form' is not an efficient cause by itself; it requires a receptive cause, and the matter and soul of Zayd are imperishable, while a new form cannot appear without a long process of development. Thus, the objector's claim does not match any actual school. Even if it did, the intellect's certainty in self-evident truths is not shaken by such remote absurdities. The passage then addresses a secondary objection about miracles: Razi notes that miracles do not involve annihilation of a permanent thing (like destroying Zayd and creating a clone), but only transformation of forms or creation of something new, which is possible. Some miracles may have figurative interpretations not elaborated here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Remote theological or philosophical possibilities can undermine self-evident knowledge.
Correction: Razi argues that such possibilities are not actual according to the relevant schools, and even if they were, the intellect's certainty in self-evident truths remains unshaken.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's response, why is the objector's claim that the first Zayd might have been annihilated and replaced not even consistent with the schools of most Muslims?
A) Because most Muslims believe bodies never perish but only compositions perish
B) Because most Muslims believe annihilation is impossible for God
C) Because most Muslims believe Zayd is recreated each moment
D) Because most Muslims believe the soul is immortal

Key Takeaway
The intellect's certainty in self-evident truths remains unshaken by unactualized remote possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a doubt in your heart about Allah's power or wisdom that you have dismissed as 'remote' but which secretly unsettles your certainty? Will you confront it by affirming that the intellect is certain and the possibility is not actual, or will you allow it to erode your faith?
?
Razi argues that remote possibilities do not shake necessary knowledge. What area of your 'aqidah do you treat as open to doubt because you entertain unlikely scenarios? Are you willing to treat those scenarios as the absurdities they are and hold fast to what you know to be true?
?
When a doubt arises in your mind that seems plausible but is based on an unactualized possibility, do you immediately become uncertain, or do you first affirm the self-evident truth and then examine the doubt's actual basis? How can you apply Razi's method in your own life starting today?

Miracles Do Not Involve Annihilation of Permanent Things
Razi answers a question about miracles in light of his refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why miracles, such as turning a stick into a serpent, do not contradict the principle that annihilation of permanent things is impossible.

Core Idea
Miracles transform forms, they do not annihilate substances.

A potential objection arises: if remote possibilities do not shake certainty, what about the miracles of the prophets, which seem to involve dramatic changes? Razi clarifies that miracles never involve the annihilation of a permanent thing (like a body's matter). Instead, they involve transforming one form into another, which is possible without annihilation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's answer
Read the Arabic passage where a question is posed about miracles, and Razi responds that there is no annihilation of a permanent thing in miracles.
The objection is a common one: if God can perform miracles, why can't He annihilate Zayd and create a copy?
2
Analyze the examples given
Razi gives examples: the stick became a serpent and returned, the she-camel from the mountain, water from the rock, reviving the dead. Note what changes and what remains.
In each case, some matter persists; only the form changes.
3
Understand the key distinction
Contrast miracles with the alleged annihilation in the doubt. Miracles are possible transformations, not annihilation of a permanent entity followed by creation of an exact likeness.
Also, some miracles have figurative interpretations that cannot be discussed here.

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the key difference between the miracles of the prophets and the hypothetical annihilation of Zayd and creation of a copy?
A) Miracles involve instantaneous creation of a likeness, while the doubt does not
B) Miracles involve annihilation of a permanent thing, while the doubt does not
C) Miracles involve transformation of forms or creation of new things, not annihilation of permanent substances

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a persistent sin in your life, do you despair that it can never be removed without destroying who you are? How does Razi's teaching that miracles transform forms—not annihilate what is permanent—give you hope that God can change your heart without erasing your identity?
?
You believe in God's power to revive the dead. But do you truly believe He can revive your deadened sense of purpose and faith? What 'rock' of hardened heart do you need to let Him crack open to bring forth living water of transformation?
?
The stick became a serpent and then returned to being a stick. Are you willing to let God change your form—your habits, your desires, your social standing—for the sake of truth, even if it means a radical transformation? What are you clinging to that you think cannot be changed without being destroyed?
