Four Objections from Absolute Deniers
Sanusi enumerates four objections from absolute deniers of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main objections raised by those who deny that reasoning can yield knowledge at all.

Core Idea
Deniers argue that reasoning leads to circularity, futility, uncertainty, or simultaneous knowledge impossibility.

After stating his proof, the author presents the arguments of those who reject reasoning entirely. These four objections challenge the very possibility of obtaining knowledge through thought. Understanding them is crucial because they represent deep skepticism that must be addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the four objections
Read the Arabic passage listing the four objections. Then read the translation. Notice each objection is a distinct logical problem.
2
Understand each objection
For each objection, identify the core claim: (1) is the result necessary or theoretical? (2) is the sought known or unknown? (3) can certainty survive later refutation? (4) can the mind hold two premises at once?
3
Connect to the proof
Reflect on how each objection targets the author's claim that reasoning yields knowledge. Note that the author will answer them later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الفكر المفيد للعلم موجود
والسمنية أنكروه مطلقا، وجمع من المهندسين اعترفوا به في العدديات والهندسيات وأنكروه في الإلهيات، وزعموا أن المقصد الأقصى فيها الأخذ بالأولى والأليق، أما الجزم فلا سبيل إليه.
لنا: أن كل واحد من مقدمة المثال المذكورة يقيني وقد يجتمعان في الذهن اجتماعا مستلزما للنتيجة المذكورة، فالنظر المفيد للعلم موجود.
احتج المنكرون للنظر مطلقا بأمور أربعة:
أولها: العلم بأن الاعتقاد الحاصل عقيب النظر علم لا يجوز أن يكون ضروريا إذ كثيرا ينكشف الأمر بخلافه، ولا نظريا وإلا لزم التسلسل وهو محال.
ثانيها: أن المطلوب إن كان معلوما فلا فائدة في طلبه، وإلا فإذا وجده كيف يعرف أنه مطلوبه.
ثالثها: أن الإنسان قد يكون مصرا على صحة دليل زمانا مديدا ثم يظهر له بعد ذلك ضعفه بدليل ثان، وذلك الاحتمال قائم في ذلك الثاني، ومع قيام الاحتمال لا يحصل اليقين.
رابعها: أن العلم بالمقدمتين لا يحصل معا في الذهن بدليل أنا نجد في أنفسنا أنا متى وجهنا الذهن نحو استحضار معلوم نقدر عليه في تلك الحالة توجيهه نحو استحضار معلوم آخر، فالحاضر في الذهن أبدا ليس إلا العلم بمقدمة واحدة وذلك غير منتج بالاتفاق فالفكر لا يفيد العلم.
احتج المنكرون النظر في الإلهيات بوجهين:
أحدهما: أن إمكان طلب التصديق موقوف على تصور الموضوع والمحمول والحقائق الإلهية غير متصورة. لنا: ما سبق أنا لا نتصور إلا ما نجده بحواسنا أو نفوسنا أو عقولنا، وإذا فقد التصور الذي هو شرط التصديق امتنع التصديق أيضا.
الجواب عن الأول: أنه نظري والتسلسل عنه لازم، لأن لزوم النتيجة عن المقدمتين إذا كان ضروريا كانتا ضروريتين إما ابتداء أو بواسطة شأنها كذلك، وعنده علم ضروري بأن اللازم عن الضروري ضروري. علم بالضرورة أن الحاصل علم.
وعن الثاني: أنه معلوم التصور مجهول التصديق والمطلوب هو التصديق، فإذا وجده ميزه عن غيره بالتصور المعلوم.
وعن الثالث: أنه معارض بأغلاط الحس.
وعن الرابع: أنا قد نعقل القضية الشرطية وهي مركبة من جملتين، والحكم بلزوم إحدى الجانبين الأخرى يستدعي حضور العلم بهما حال الحكم بذلك اللزوم، وذلك يدل على إمكان اجتماع العلمين دفعة في الذهن.
ومن الخامس: هب أن تلك الماهيات غير متصورة بحسب حقائقها لكنها متصورة بحسب عوارضها المشتركة بينها وبين المحدثات وذلك كافٍ في إمكان التصديق.
وعن السادس: أن ما ذكرتموه يدل على صعوبة تحصيل هذا العلم لا على تعذره.
Issue: The thought that yields knowledge exists.
The Samaniyya denied it absolutely, and a group of the engineers acknowledged it in arithmetic and geometry but denied it in divine matters, claiming that the ultimate aim in them is to adopt the most appropriate and fitting, but certainty has no path to it.
Our proof: Each of the two premises of the mentioned example is certain, and they may come together in the mind in a conjunction that entails the mentioned conclusion. Thus, the reasoning that yields knowledge exists.
The deniers of reasoning absolutely objected with four things:
First: The knowledge that the belief attained after reasoning is such that it cannot be necessary, for often the matter is revealed to be contrary to it, nor theoretical, otherwise circularity would follow, which is impossible.
Second: If the sought is known, then there is no benefit in seeking it; but if it is not known, then when he finds it, how does he know that it is his sought?
Third: A person may be insistent on the correctness of a proof for a long time, then later its weakness appears to him through a second proof, and that possibility remains in the second one, and with the possibility remaining certainty is not obtained.
Fourth: Knowledge of the two premises does not occur together in the mind, evidenced by the fact that we find in ourselves that when we direct the mind toward summoning one known thing we are able to direct it toward summoning another, so what is present in the mind at any moment is only knowledge of one premise, and that is not productive by agreement, so thought does not yield knowledge.
The deniers of reasoning in divine matters objected with two arguments:
First: The possibility of seeking assent depends on conceiving the subject and predicate, and divine realities are not conceivable. [Objector says:] We have what preceded: we only conceive what we find through our senses, our selves, or our intellects, and when the conception which is a condition of assent is absent, assent is also impossible.
The answer to the first: It is theoretical, and circularity is not entailed because if the entailment of the conclusion from the two premises is necessary, then they are both necessary, either immediately or through an intermediary with the same property, and he has necessary knowledge that what is entailed by the necessary is necessary. It is known necessarily that what is obtained is knowledge.
And to the second: It is known in conception but unknown in assent, and the sought is assent, so when he finds it he distinguishes it from others by the known conception.
And to the third: It is countered by errors of the senses.
And to the fourth: We may understand a conditional proposition, which is composed of two statements, and the judgment of the entailment of one side from the other requires the presence of knowledge of both at the time of judging that entailment, and that indicates the possibility of the two knowledges coming together in the mind at once.
And to the fifth: Suppose those essences are not conceivable in terms of their true realities, but they are conceivable in terms of their common accidents between them and the contingent things, and that is sufficient for the possibility of assent.
And to the sixth: What you have mentioned indicates the difficulty of obtaining this knowledge, not its impossibility.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation sequence. It begins by stating the issue (mas'ala) that 'thought that yields knowledge exists'. The Samaniyya (a sect) denied it altogether, while some mathematicians acknowledged it in mathematics but denied it in theology, claiming that only plausible opinions are possible there, not certainty. The author then gives a brief proof: if two certain premises come together in the mind in a way that entails a certain conclusion, that process yields knowledge. Next, he lists four objections from absolute deniers of reasoning: (1) the belief after reasoning is neither necessary nor theoretical (to avoid circularity); (2) if the sought is known, seeking is futile, if unknown, one cannot recognize it; (3) a long-held proof may later be shown false, so doubt persists; (4) the mind cannot hold both premises simultaneously, so no inference occurs. Then two objections from deniers of theological reasoning: (5) divine essences are inconceivable, so assent is impossible; (6) even the self (the 'I') is disputed, how then can we know the hidden? The author then provides six answers in order: to (1) he distinguishes necessary entailment from circularity; to (2) he distinguishes conception from assent; to (3) he notes that sensory errors also occur but do not invalidate all sense perception; to (4) he gives the example of conditional propositions requiring simultaneous knowledge; to (5) he admits that divine essences are not directly conceivable but are known through their attributes common with contingent things; to (6) he says this only shows difficulty, not impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief that all reasoning must eventually lead to infinite regress.
Correction: The author will later show that necessary premises can end the regress.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection?
A) Reasoning leads to infinite regress.
B) If the sought is known, seeking is futile; if unknown, it cannot be recognized.
C) The mind cannot hold two premises at once.
D) Later refutation undermines certainty.

Key Takeaway
The four objections challenge reasoning on grounds of circularity, futility, uncertainty, and mental simultaneity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read objection two—that if you already know something, seeking is futile, and if you don't know it, you can't recognize it—does this reflect your own hesitation in studying proofs about Allah? Have you ever felt that you either already believe or you'll never be convinced? What does that feeling say about your trust in reasoning? Will you commit to seeking knowledge with the hope that you can recognize truth when it comes?
?
Objection three warns that a proof you hold for years can later be shown weak. Have you ever abandoned a belief because someone presented a seemingly stronger counter-argument? In that moment, did you realize that your certainty was fragile? What step will you take today to ground your faith in a proof that cannot be shaken, rather than just following what feels convincing?
?
The fourth objection says the mind cannot hold both premises of an argument at once, so inference is impossible. When you try to reflect on theological proofs, do you find your mind wandering or forgetting key steps? Does that make you doubt the validity of reasoning itself? On the Day of Judgment, will you excuse your ignorance by saying 'I couldn't hold two thoughts at once'? How will you train your mind to focus so that you can know your Creator with certainty?

Two Objections from Deniers of Theological Reasoning
Sanusi presents two objections from deniers of reasoning in divine matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific objections raised against using reasoning in divine matters.

Core Idea
Critics argue that divine essences are inconceivable, so reasoning about them is impossible.

In addition to the four general objections, some thinkers specifically deny reasoning in theology. They claim that divine realities cannot be conceived, making assent impossible. The author will later respond, but first we need to understand these objections clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that the first objection concerns inconceivability of divine essences, and the second concerns the unknowability of the self.
2
Analyze the first objection
The first objection argues that assent depends on conception, and divine essences are inconceivable because they are not sensed, self-evident, or rationally grasped.
3
Analyze the second objection
The second objection points to the difficulty of knowing the self, let alone higher realities, implying that theological knowledge is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the objection that divine essences are inconceivable, do you ever feel a moment of doubt? How does your heart respond to the challenge that reason may be insufficient in matters of faith? ?

In light of this lesson, are you ready to trust that divine revelation provides the necessary conception that reason alone cannot reach?
?
Sanusi presents these objections not as straw men but as real challenges. Do you have the courage to face the strongest arguments against theological reasoning? If you are honest, do you sometimes avoid difficult questions about the nature of God? This lesson calls you to engage, not to ignore. Will you commit to seeking knowledge through both reason and revelation?

Refutation of All Six Objections
Sanusi provides refutations for the six objections against beneficial reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes each of the six objections against beneficial reasoning.

Core Idea
Each objection is answered by showing that reasoning avoids circularity, is not futile, can yield certainty, and can handle simultaneous premises and divine concepts.

The author now provides systematic answers to the six objections. He addresses the four general objections first, then the two theological ones. These refutations are crucial for defending the validity of rational thought in all domains, including theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the six answers
Read the Arabic passage containing the six answers. Then read the translation. Note that each answer corresponds to the objection in order.
2
Understand each refutation
For each answer, identify the key point: (1) necessary premises avoid regress; (2) distinction between conception and assent; (3) sensory errors do not invalidate all sense; (4) conditional propositions require simultaneous knowledge; (5) divine essences known through attributes; (6) difficulty is not impossibility.
3
Assess the overall defense
Consider how the author's responses collectively defend the possibility of reasoning yielding knowledge, especially in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الفكر المفيد للعلم موجود
والسمنية أنكروه مطلقا، وجمع من المهندسين اعترفوا به في العدديات والهندسيات وأنكروه في الإلهيات، وزعموا أن المقصد الأقصى فيها الأخذ بالأولى والأليق، أما الجزم فلا سبيل إليه.
لنا: أن كل واحد من مقدمة المثال المذكورة يقيني وقد يجتمعان في الذهن اجتماعا مستلزما للنتيجة المذكورة، فالنظر المفيد للعلم موجود.
احتج المنكرون للنظر مطلقا بأمور أربعة:
أولها: العلم بأن الاعتقاد الحاصل عقيب النظر علم لا يجوز أن يكون ضروريا إذ كثيرا ينكشف الأمر بخلافه، ولا نظريا وإلا لزم التسلسل وهو محال.
ثانيها: أن المطلوب إن كان معلوما فلا فائدة في طلبه، وإلا فإذا وجده كيف يعرف أنه مطلوبه.
ثالثها: أن الإنسان قد يكون مصرا على صحة دليل زمانا مديدا ثم يظهر له بعد ذلك ضعفه بدليل ثان، وذلك الاحتمال قائم في ذلك الثاني، ومع قيام الاحتمال لا يحصل اليقين.
رابعها: أن العلم بالمقدمتين لا يحصل معا في الذهن بدليل أنا نجد في أنفسنا أنا متى وجهنا الذهن نحو استحضار معلوم نقدر عليه في تلك الحالة توجيهه نحو استحضار معلوم آخر، فالحاضر في الذهن أبدا ليس إلا العلم بمقدمة واحدة وذلك غير منتج بالاتفاق فالفكر لا يفيد العلم.
احتج المنكرون النظر في الإلهيات بوجهين:
أحدهما: أن إمكان طلب التصديق موقوف على تصور الموضوع والمحمول والحقائق الإلهية غير متصورة. لنا: ما سبق أنا لا نتصور إلا ما نجده بحواسنا أو نفوسنا أو عقولنا، وإذا فقد التصور الذي هو شرط التصديق امتنع التصديق أيضا.
الجواب عن الأول: أنه نظري والتسلسل عنه لازم، لأن لزوم النتيجة عن المقدمتين إذا كان ضروريا كانتا ضروريتين إما ابتداء أو بواسطة شأنها كذلك، وعنده علم ضروري بأن اللازم عن الضروري ضروري. علم بالضرورة أن الحاصل علم.
وعن الثاني: أنه معلوم التصور مجهول التصديق والمطلوب هو التصديق، فإذا وجده ميزه عن غيره بالتصور المعلوم.
وعن الثالث: أنه معارض بأغلاط الحس.
وعن الرابع: أنا قد نعقل القضية الشرطية وهي مركبة من جملتين، والحكم بلزوم إحدى الجانبين الأخرى يستدعي حضور العلم بهما حال الحكم بذلك اللزوم، وذلك يدل على إمكان اجتماع العلمين دفعة في الذهن.
ومن الخامس: هب أن تلك الماهيات غير متصورة بحسب حقائقها لكنها متصورة بحسب عوارضها المشتركة بينها وبين المحدثات وذلك كافٍ في إمكان التصديق.
وعن السادس: أن ما ذكرتموه يدل على صعوبة تحصيل هذا العلم لا على تعذره.
Issue: The thought that yields knowledge exists.
The Samaniyya denied it absolutely, and a group of the engineers acknowledged it in arithmetic and geometry but denied it in divine matters, claiming that the ultimate aim in them is to adopt the most appropriate and fitting, but certainty has no path to it.
Our proof: Each of the two premises of the mentioned example is certain, and they may come together in the mind in a conjunction that entails the mentioned conclusion. Thus, the reasoning that yields knowledge exists.
The deniers of reasoning absolutely objected with four things:
First: The knowledge that the belief attained after reasoning is such that it cannot be necessary, for often the matter is revealed to be contrary to it, nor theoretical, otherwise circularity would follow, which is impossible.
Second: If the sought is known, then there is no benefit in seeking it; but if it is not known, then when he finds it, how does he know that it is his sought?
Third: A person may be insistent on the correctness of a proof for a long time, then later its weakness appears to him through a second proof, and that possibility remains in the second one, and with the possibility remaining certainty is not obtained.
Fourth: Knowledge of the two premises does not occur together in the mind, evidenced by the fact that we find in ourselves that when we direct the mind toward summoning one known thing we are able to direct it toward summoning another, so what is present in the mind at any moment is only knowledge of one premise, and that is not productive by agreement, so thought does not yield knowledge.
The deniers of reasoning in divine matters objected with two arguments:
First: The possibility of seeking assent depends on conceiving the subject and predicate, and divine realities are not conceivable. [Objector says:] We have what preceded: we only conceive what we find through our senses, our selves, or our intellects, and when the conception which is a condition of assent is absent, assent is also impossible.
The answer to the first: It is theoretical, and circularity is not entailed because if the entailment of the conclusion from the two premises is necessary, then they are both necessary, either immediately or through an intermediary with the same property, and he has necessary knowledge that what is entailed by the necessary is necessary. It is known necessarily that what is obtained is knowledge.
And to the second: It is known in conception but unknown in assent, and the sought is assent, so when he finds it he distinguishes it from others by the known conception.
And to the third: It is countered by errors of the senses.
And to the fourth: We may understand a conditional proposition, which is composed of two statements, and the judgment of the entailment of one side from the other requires the presence of knowledge of both at the time of judging that entailment, and that indicates the possibility of the two knowledges coming together in the mind at once.
And to the fifth: Suppose those essences are not conceivable in terms of their true realities, but they are conceivable in terms of their common accidents between them and the contingent things, and that is sufficient for the possibility of assent.
And to the sixth: What you have mentioned indicates the difficulty of obtaining this knowledge, not its impossibility.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation sequence. It begins by stating the issue (mas'ala) that 'thought that yields knowledge exists'. The Samaniyya (a sect) denied it altogether, while some mathematicians acknowledged it in mathematics but denied it in theology, claiming that only plausible opinions are possible there, not certainty. The author then gives a brief proof: if two certain premises come together in the mind in a way that entails a certain conclusion, that process yields knowledge. Next, he lists four objections from absolute deniers of reasoning: (1) the belief after reasoning is neither necessary nor theoretical (to avoid circularity); (2) if the sought is known, seeking is futile, if unknown, one cannot recognize it; (3) a long-held proof may later be shown false, so doubt persists; (4) the mind cannot hold both premises simultaneously, so no inference occurs. Then two objections from deniers of theological reasoning: (5) divine essences are inconceivable, so assent is impossible; (6) even the self (the 'I') is disputed, how then can we know the hidden? The author then provides six answers in order: to (1) he distinguishes necessary entailment from circularity; to (2) he distinguishes conception from assent; to (3) he notes that sensory errors also occur but do not invalidate all sense perception; to (4) he gives the example of conditional propositions requiring simultaneous knowledge; to (5) he admits that divine essences are not directly conceivable but are known through their attributes common with contingent things; to (6) he says this only shows difficulty, not impossibility.

Key Terms

الضروري
التصور
التصديق
القضية الشرطية
العوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the first objection proves infinite regress is unavoidable.
Correction: The author shows that necessary premises can terminate the regress.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that the mind cannot hold two premises at once?
A) By denying the objection
B) By citing conditional propositions that require simultaneous knowledge
C) By appealing to divine grace
D) By saying it's rare but possible

Key Takeaway
All six objections are refuted, showing that reasoning can yield certain knowledge, even in theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a reasoned argument about Allah because it required an intermediate step? The first answer affirms that necessary knowledge can be mediated. Is your heart patient enough to follow a chain of reasoning, or do you demand immediate conclusions? Will you commit to studying proofs that require patience?
?
Consider the fourth answer: two knowledges can be present simultaneously. In your own search for certainty, do you allow multiple evidences to coexist in your heart, or do you insist on a single, simple proof? What does that say about your trust in the complexity Allah has placed in creation and revelation? Are you willing to embrace intellectual complexity for the sake of deeper faith?
?
The sixth refutation says difficulty does not equal impossibility. Think of an aspect of Islamic theology you find difficult to understand. Have you used that difficulty as an excuse to avoid seeking knowledge? What would change if you treated it as a challenge to overcome rather than a barrier? Will you take one concrete step today to study that topic despite its difficulty?

Breaking Circularity: The First Samaniyya Objection
Commentator summarizes the answer to the Samaniyya's first objection about circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessity of the conclusion from two necessary premises breaks the alleged circularity in reasoning.

Core Idea
Necessity from two necessary premises yields immediate necessary knowledge, breaking any infinite regress.

The Samaniyya argued that reasoning leads to an infinite regress. In this passage, the commentator summarizes the key response: the conclusion of a valid syllogism is necessarily entailed by its premises, and whatever is necessarily entailed by two necessary premises is itself known necessarily. This stops the regress because the necessity is immediate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises of the answer
Read the passage and find the two premises that together prove the conclusion is necessary.
2
Understand how the regress is broken
The conclusion is known intuitively from its conception, so no further syllogism is needed.
3
Reflect on the importance of necessary premises
The premises themselves are necessary, ensuring the conclusion is also necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: حاصل الجواب عن أول شبه السمنية أن العلم بأن نتيجة القياس المفروض علم نظري حاصل من مقدمتين إحداهما أن تلك النتيجة لازمة بالضرورة عن المقدمتين، وهذه المقدمة ظاهرة البيان كما ذكره في المنطق، وثانيتهما أن كل لازم بالضرورة عن ضروريتين علم بالضرورة، فإذا نتيجة القياس المفروض علم بالضرورة بديهي يحصل من نفس تصورها فينقطع التسلسل.
والجواب عن ثاني شبههم كما ذكر.
وأما الجواب عن ثالثها وهو المعارضة بغلط الحس: فالحاصل منه أن الحس يغلط مع أنكم معترفون بحكمه حقا فخلط العقل أيضا مثله، فإما أن يعترفوا بحقيقة أحكام العقل وإما أن ينكروا حقيقة أحكام الحس وهذا جواب جدلي.
والجواب الحق: أن وقوع الغلط في البعض مع جواز الاحتراز عنه لا يوجب رد الكل والاحتمال غير باق مع جزم العقل.
وعن رابعها وخامسها: كما ذكر.
I say: The summary of the answer to the first objection of the Samaniyya is that the knowledge that the result of the supposed syllogism is theoretical knowledge is obtained from two premises: one of them is that that result is necessarily entailed by the two premises, and this premise is clear as mentioned in logic; the second is that everything necessarily entailed by two necessary premises is known necessarily. Hence, the result of the supposed syllogism is known necessarily, intuitively, obtained from its very conception, so the circularity is broken.
And the answer to their second objection is as mentioned.
As for the answer to their third, which is the counter by sensory error: the gist of it is that the senses err while you acknowledge their judgment as true, so the error of the intellect is likewise. So either they acknowledge the truth of intellectual judgments or they deny the truth of sensory judgments, and this is a dialectical answer.
And the true answer: That the occurrence of error in some cases, while avoidance is possible, does not necessitate rejecting all, and the probability does not exist when the mind is certain.
And to their fourth and fifth: as mentioned.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's gloss on the previous responses, summarizing and clarifying the answers to the Samaniyya's first, third, and other objections. The commentator begins by summarizing the answer to the first objection (regarding circularity): the key is that the conclusion is necessarily entailed by two necessary premises, and whatever is necessarily entailed by two necessary premises is itself necessary. This breaks the potential circularity because the necessity is immediate from conception. For the second objection, he simply refers to the earlier answer. For the third objection (the possibility of error in reasoning is compared to sensory error), he explains two answers: a dialectical one (if you accept sensory judgments despite errors, you must accept intellectual judgments similarly) and a true answer (occasional error with possible avoidance does not invalidate all certainty, and when the mind is certain, probability does not remain). For the fourth and fifth objections (about simultaneous premises and inconceivable essences), he just refers to earlier answers.

Key Terms
النتيجة
الضروريتين
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the conclusion of the syllogism not lead to an infinite regress?
A) Because the conclusion is the same as the premises
B) Because the conclusion is known necessarily from its very conception
C) Because the premises are uncertain
D) Because the regress stops at the senses

Key Takeaway
The necessity of the conclusion from two necessary premises makes it immediately known, halting any infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Samaniyya argued that reasoning leads to an infinite regress of proofs. Have you ever felt that your faith in Allah requires an endless chain of reasons to be certain? What does this lesson say about the possibility of immediate, necessary knowledge that stops that chain? How will you seek that intuitive certainty today?
?
This passage teaches that a conclusion can be known necessarily without further proof if its premises are necessary. Reflect on a basic belief you hold about your Creator: do you treat it as merely theoretical, needing constant reinforcement, or as intuitively necessary? What concrete step can you take to move from the former to the latter?
?
The commentator says the conclusion is known 'from its very conception.' Are there truths about Allah, the Hereafter, or your own accountability that you have conceptually accepted but not felt as immediately necessary? If the circularity of doubt is broken only when knowledge becomes intuitive, what is holding you back from making that knowledge a felt certainty in your daily life?

Error in Reasoning or Senses? The Samaniyya's Third Objection
Commentator summarizes the answers to the Samaniyya's third objection about sensory error analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two answers to the objection that reasoning can be erroneous like the senses: a dialectical answer and a true answer.

Core Idea
Occasional error does not invalidate certainty when proper safeguards are taken.

The Samaniyya argued that since reasoning can err like the senses, we cannot trust it. The commentator presents two responses: a dialectical one that forces consistency, and a true one that distinguishes between occasional error and genuine certainty when safeguards are observed.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two answers
Identify the dialectical answer and the true answer in the passage.
Dialectical: consistency with their acceptance of sensory error. True: occasional error does not reject all.
2
Understand the dialectical response
If they accept sensory judgments despite sensory error, they must also accept intellectual judgments despite possible error.
3
Understand the true response
Error in some cases with possible avoidance does not necessitate rejecting all judgments, and when the mind is certain, no probability of error remains.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any possibility of error makes all reasoning unreliable.

The Samaniyya Skepticism on Knowledge of the Self
Razi quotes the second Samaniyya objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Samaniyya objection that disagreement about the self undermines the possibility of certain knowledge of hidden things.

Core Idea
Disagreement about even the self casts doubt on all knowledge.

The Samaniyya skeptics challenge the possibility of certain knowledge by pointing to fundamental disagreements about the most immediate object of awareness: the self. They argue that if even the clearest thing is disputed, then knowledge of hidden things is impossible. This lesson presents their objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the Samaniyya objection. Notice the list of contradictory views about the self.
The objection uses 'huwiyya' for self, a key term.
2
Identify the different views
List the five views mentioned about what the self is. Think about how each view is mutually incompatible.
Views range from physical body to rational soul.
3
Understand the skeptical conclusion
The objection concludes that if the most apparent thing is contested, knowledge of hidden things is hopeless. Reflect on the strength of this skeptical challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما : أن أظهر الأشياء للإنسان وأقربها منه هويته التى إليها يشير بقوله أنا ، ثم إن العقلاء اختلفوا في تلك الهوية اختلافا لا يكاد يمكن الجزم بواحد منها فيهم من قال هى هذا الهيكل المحسوس ، ومنهم من قال أجسام سارية فيه ، ومنهم من قال جزء لا يتجزأ في القلب ، ومنهم من قال المزاج ، ومنهم من قال النفس الناطقة . وإذا كان علم الإنسان بأظهر الأمور وأقربها منه كذلك فكيف يكون حاله في معرفة أخفى الأمور وأبعدها مناسبة عنه الجواب عن الأول : أنه نظری و التسلسل عنه لازم ، لأن لزوم النتيجة عن المقدمتين إذا كان ضروريا كانتا ضروريتين إما ابتداء أو بواسطة شأنها كذلك ، وعنده علم ضرورى بأن الازم عن الضروري ضروري . علم بالضرورة أن الحاصل علم . وعن الثاني : أنه معلوم التصور مجهول التصديق والمطلوب هو التصديق ، فإذا وجده ميزه عن غيره بالنصور المعلوم . وعن الثالث : أنه معارض بأغلاط الحس . وعن الرابع : أنا قد نعقل القضية الشرطية وهى مركبة من جملتين ، والحكم بلزوم إحدى الجوانين الأخرى يستدعى حضور العلم بهما حال الحكم بذلك اللزوم، وذلك يدل على إمكان اجتماع العلمين دفعة في الذهن . ومن الخامس : هب أن تلك الماهيات غير متهورة بحسب حقائقها لكنها متصورة بحسب عوارضها المشتركة بينهما وبية المحدثات وذلك كاف في إمكان التصديق . وعن السادس : أن ما ذكرتموه بدل على صعوبة تحصيل هذا العلم لا على تعذره (1) أقول : حاصل الجواب عن أقل شبه السمنية أن العلم بأن نتيجة القياس المفروض علم نظری حاصل من مقدمتين إحد يهما أن تلك النتيجة لازمة بالضرورة الضرورتين وهذه المقدمة ظاهرة البيان كما ذكره في المنطق ، وثانيتهما أن كل لازم بالضرورة لضرورتين علم بالضرورة ، فإذا نتيجة القياس المفروض علم بالضرورة بديهى يحصل من نفس تصورها فينقطع التسلسل والجواب عن ثاني شبههم كما ذكر . وأما الجواب عن ثالثها وهو المعارضة بغلط الحس : فالحاصل منه أن الحس يغلط مع أنكم معترفون يكون حكمة حقا فخلط العقل أيضا مثله ، فإما أن يعترفوا بحقيقة أحكام العقل وإما أن ينكروا حقيقة أحكام الحس وهذا جواب جدلى . والجواب الحق : أن وقوع الغلط في البعض مع جواز الاحتراز عنه لا يوجب رد الكل والاحتمالي غير باق مع جزم العقل . وعن رابعها وخامسها : كما ذكر .
And the second: That the most apparent thing to man and the closest to him is his self (huwiyya), to which he points by saying 'I'. Then the rational people differed concerning that self in a disagreement that one can hardly be certain of any one view. Among them are those who say it is this sensible structure; among them are those who say it is subtle bodies permeating it; among them are those who say it is an indivisible part in the heart; among them are those who say it is the temperament; among them are those who say it is the rational soul. If man's knowledge of the most apparent and closest things is like this, then how would his situation be in knowing the most hidden and farthest things? The answer to the first: that it is theoretical and regress is necessary from it, because if the entailment of the conclusion from the two premises is necessary, then the two premises are necessary, either originally or by mediation whose state is likewise. And he holds the necessary knowledge that what is entailed by the necessary is necessary. He knows necessarily that what is obtained is knowledge. And about the second: that it is known in conception (tasawwur) but unknown in affirmation (tasdiq), and the goal is affirmation. So if he finds it, he distinguishes it from others by the known conception. And about the third: that it is countered by errors of the senses. And about the fourth: that we can conceive the conditional proposition, which is composed of two sentences, and the judgment of the entailment of one of the two sides to the other requires the presence of knowledge of both at the time of judging that entailment, and that indicates the possibility of two items of knowledge coexisting simultaneously in the mind. And from the fifth: Even if those essences are not determined according to their realities, they are conceived according to their common accidents between them and originated things, and that suffices for the possibility of affirmation. And about the sixth: that what you have mentioned indicates the difficulty of acquiring this knowledge, not its impossibility. (1) I say: The summary of the answer to the least of the Samaniyya's doubts is that the knowledge that the result of the supposed syllogism is theoretical knowledge derived from two premises, one of which is that that result is necessarily entailed by the two necessities, and this premise is evident as mentioned in logic, and the second is that everything necessarily entailed by two necessities is known necessarily. Thus the result of the supposed syllogism is necessary knowledge, intuitive, obtained from its very conception, so the regress is cut. And the answer to their second doubt is as mentioned. As for the answer to the third of them, which is the counter-objection by sensory error: the conclusion from it is that the sense errs while you acknowledge that its judgment is true, so the intellect also errs like it. So either they acknowledge the truth of the intellect's judgments or they deny the truth of the senses' judgments. This is a dialectical answer. And the true answer: that the occurrence of error in some, with the possibility of avoiding it, does not necessitate rejection of the whole, and the probability is not remaining when the intellect is certain. And about the fourth and fifth of them: as mentioned.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection of the Samaniyya skeptics and then gives a series of answers to objections raised (presumably the first through sixth). The objection is that even the most obvious thing for a person—his own self—is subject to irreconcilable disagreements: some say it is the physical body, others subtle bodies, an indivisible part, the temperament, or the rational soul. If something so obvious is disputed, knowledge of hidden things becomes impossible. The author then answers each objection in order. To the first (which states that the argument leads to regress), he responds that the conclusion is theoretical, and the regress is cut by a necessary premise that what is entailed by necessary premises is known necessarily. To the second (about the conclusion being unknown in affirmation), he says the conclusion is known in conception but unknown in affirmation; the goal is affirmation, and once found, it is distinguished. To the third (countered by sensory errors), he gives a dialectical answer: if the senses can err while being accepted, so can the intellect; the skeptic must either accept both or reject both. The true answer is that isolated errors with possible safeguards do not invalidate all judgments. To the fourth (about impossibility of two knowledges simultaneously), he argues that we do conceive conditional propositions, and the entailment judgment requires awareness of both premises, proving co-occurrence is possible. To the fifth (essences not determined), he says their common accidental attributes suffice for affirmation. To the sixth (difficulty implies impossibility), he distinguishes difficulty from impossibility. The author then summarizes a key refutation: the knowledge from a syllogism is intuitive and necessary, breaking the regress.

Key Terms
هوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disagreement implies something is unknowable.
Correction: Disagreement does not entail impossibility of certain knowledge; it only shows difficulty or the need for careful reasoning.

Checkpoint
How many distinct views about the self does the Samaniyya objection list?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The Samaniyya objection uses disagreement about the self to argue that certain knowledge is unattainable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been absolutely certain about something about yourself, only to later realize you were mistaken? How does that experience affect your confidence in your own self-knowledge?
?
The Samaniyya use disagreement about the self to cast doubt on all knowledge. Have you ever allowed differing opinions on religious matters to shake your own convictions? What did you do in that moment of uncertainty?
?
What is the most immediate thing you know about your own self—your body, your thoughts, your soul, or something else? How does this lesson push you to seek a deeper, divinely grounded certainty about who you are before Allah?

Rational Judgments
advanced
10 min
Razi's Refutation of the Samaniyya's Objections
Razi provides six answers and a summary refutation to the Samaniyya's objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's six answers to the Samaniyya objections and his conclusive refutation that knowledge from syllogism is intuitive and breaks the regress.

Core Idea
Razi systematically defends the possibility of certain knowledge against skeptical objections.

Razi counters the Samaniyya's challenge by addressing each of their objections one by one. He shows that theoretical knowledge does not lead to infinite regress, sensory error does not invalidate all intellect, and difficulty does not imply impossibility. His summary refutation presents a strong positive argument for certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the six answers sequentially
Read the Arabic and translation of each answer (1-6). Notice how Razi uses logical and epistemological arguments.
Each answer targets a different aspect of the skeptic's challenge.
2
Understand the first answer on regress
The first answer argues that the conclusion from necessary premises is itself necessary, stopping the regress. Grasp this reasoning.
Key: necessary from necessary is necessary.
3
Examine the dialectical and true answers
The third answer has a dialectical part and a true part. Contrast them. The true answer shows that occasional error does not invalidate all judgment.
4
Analyze the summary refutation
Read the 'I say' paragraph. Razi summarizes: the knowledge from syllogism is intuitive and necessary, cutting off the regress. This is the core refutation.
This paragraph wraps up the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما : أن أظهر الأشياء للإنسان وأقربها منه هويته التى إليها يشير بقوله أنا ، ثم إن العقلاء اختلفوا في تلك الهوية اختلافا لا يكاد يمكن الجزم بواحد منها فيهم من قال هى هذا الهيكل المحسوس ، ومنهم من قال أجسام سارية فيه ، ومنهم من قال جزء لا يتجزأ في القلب ، ومنهم من قال المزاج ، ومنهم من قال النفس الناطقة . وإذا كان علم الإنسان بأظهر الأمور وأقربها منه كذلك فكيف يكون حاله في معرفة أخفى الأمور وأبعدها مناسبة عنه الجواب عن الأول : أنه نظری و التسلسل عنه لازم ، لأن لزوم النتيجة عن المقدمتين إذا كان ضروريا كانتا ضروريتين إما ابتداء أو بواسطة شأنها كذلك ، وعنده علم ضرورى بأن الازم عن الضروري ضروري . علم بالضرورة أن الحاصل علم . وعن الثاني : أنه معلوم التصور مجهول التصديق والمطلوب هو التصديق ، فإذا وجده ميزه عن غيره بالنصور المعلوم . وعن الثالث : أنه معارض بأغلاط الحس . وعن الرابع : أنا قد نعقل القضية الشرطية وهى مركبة من جملتين ، والحكم بلزوم إحدى الجوانين الأخرى يستدعى حضور العلم بهما حال الحكم بذلك اللزوم، وذلك يدل على إمكان اجتماع العلمين دفعة في الذهن . ومن الخامس : هب أن تلك الماهيات غير متهورة بحسب حقائقها لكنها متصورة بحسب عوارضها المشتركة بينهما وبية المحدثات وذلك كاف في إمكان التصديق . وعن السادس : أن ما ذكرتموه بدل على صعوبة تحصيل هذا العلم لا على تعذره (1) أقول : حاصل الجواب عن أقل شبه السمنية أن العلم بأن نتيجة القياس المفروض علم نظری حاصل من مقدمتين إحد يهما أن تلك النتيجة لازمة بالضرورة الضرورتين وهذه المقدمة ظاهرة البيان كما ذكره في المنطق ، وثانيتهما أن كل لازم بالضرورة لضرورتين علم بالضرورة ، فإذا نتيجة القياس المفروض علم بالضرورة بديهى يحصل من نفس تصورها فينقطع التسلسل والجواب عن ثاني شبههم كما ذكر . وأما الجواب عن ثالثها وهو المعارضة بغلط الحس : فالحاصل منه أن الحس يغلط مع أنكم معترفون يكون حكمة حقا فخلط العقل أيضا مثله ، فإما أن يعترفوا بحقيقة أحكام العقل وإما أن ينكروا حقيقة أحكام الحس وهذا جواب جدلى . والجواب الحق : أن وقوع الغلط في البعض مع جواز الاحتراز عنه لا يوجب رد الكل والاحتمالي غير باق مع جزم العقل . وعن رابعها وخامسها : كما ذكر .
And the second: That the most apparent thing to man and the closest to him is his self (huwiyya), to which he points by saying 'I'. Then the rational people differed concerning that self in a disagreement that one can hardly be certain of any one view. Among them are those who say it is this sensible structure; among them are those who say it is subtle bodies permeating it; among them are those who say it is an indivisible part in the heart; among them are those who say it is the temperament; among them are those who say it is the rational soul. The answer to the first: that it is theoretical and regress is necessary from it, because if the entailment of the conclusion from the two premises is necessary, then the two premises are necessary, either originally or by mediation whose state is likewise. And about the second: that it is known in conception (tasawwur) but unknown in affirmation (tasdiq), and the goal is affirmation. So if he finds it, he distinguishes it from others by the known conception. And about the third: that it is countered by errors of the senses. And about the fourth: that we can conceive the conditional proposition, which is composed of two sentences, and the judgment of the entailment of one of the two sides to the other requires the presence of knowledge of both at the time of judging that entailment, and that indicates the possibility of two items of knowledge coexisting simultaneously in the mind. And from the fifth: Even if those essences are not determined according to their realities, they are conceived according to their common accidents between them and originated things, and that suffices for the possibility of affirmation. And about the sixth: that what you have mentioned indicates the difficulty of acquiring this knowledge, not its impossibility. (1) I say: The summary of the answer to the least of the Samaniyya's doubts is that the knowledge that the result of the supposed syllogism is theoretical knowledge derived from two premises, one of which is that that result is necessarily entailed by the two necessities, and this premise is evident as mentioned in logic, and the second is that everything necessarily entailed by two necessities is known necessarily. So either they acknowledge the truth of the intellect's judgments or they deny the truth of the senses' judgments. This is a dialectical answer. And the true answer: that the occurrence of error in some, with the possibility of avoiding it, does not necessitate rejection of the whole, and the probability is not remaining when the intellect is certain. And about the fourth and fifth of them: as mentioned.

Commentary

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An occasional error means all judgments are unreliable.
Correction: Razi argues that error in some cases, with possibility of avoidance, does not warrant rejection of all judgments, especially when certainty is attained.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's true answer to the third objection, why does the occurrence of error not invalidate all intellectual judgments?
A) The intellect never errs.
B) Error can be avoided, and certainty removes remaining probability.
C) The senses are always correct.
D) Dialectical argument forces acceptance.

Key Takeaway
Razi's six answers and summary refutation collectively defend the possibility of certain knowledge against skeptical challenges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doubt that seems to undermine your certainty in a fundamental truth of Islam, do you immediately dismiss the doubt or do you engage with it systematically as Razi does? What would it cost you to follow Razi's method in that moment?
?
Razi distinguishes between difficulty and impossibility. Is there an area of your faith where you have mistaken difficulty for impossibility, thereby abandoning the pursuit of certainty? What step can you take today to move past that difficulty?
?
The summary shows that certain knowledge can be obtained from self-evident premises. Examine your own heart: do you truly believe that the foundational truths of Islam are self-evident, or have you allowed skepticism to erode that conviction? What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that certainty?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
The Ash'ari Position on the Obligation of Rational Reflection
Razi states the Ash'ari proof against rational obligation of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that rational reflection (nazar) is not obligatory by reason alone, based on the verse 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger.'

Core Idea
Reason cannot determine obligation; only revelation can establish it.

This lesson presents the Ash'ari proof that rational reflection (nazar) for attaining knowledge of God is not obligatory by reason, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The argument hinges on the fact that obligation depends on reward and punishment, which cannot be rationally determined because nothing God does is morally ugly (qabih). Instead, obligation is known through revelation alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice how the issue is framed and the proof is given.
2
Identify the key premise
The proof relies on the verse and the claim that nothing God does is qabih. Why does this make rational obligation impossible?
3
Reflect on the implication
If reason cannot establish obligation, then what is the basis for the obligation to know God? The answer is revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : وجوب النظر سممى خلافا للمتزلة وبعض الفقهاء من الشافعية والحنفية) لنا قوله تعالى : وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا ، ولأن فائدة الوجوب الثواب والعقاب ، ولا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء من أفعاله ، فلا يمكن القطع بالثواب والعقاب من جهة العقل ، فلا يمكن القطع بالوجوب .
واحتجوا : بأنه لو لم يثبت الوجوب إلا بالسمع الذى لا يعلم صحته إلا بالنظر فللمخاطب أن يقول : إنى لا أنظر حتى لا أعرف كون السمع صدقا وذلك مفض إلى إلام الأنبياء
والجواب : أن هذا لازم عليكم أيضاً لأن وجوب النظر وإن كان عندكم عقليا لكنه غير معلوم بضرورة العقل ، لا أن العلم بوجوب النظر يتوقف عند المعتزلة على العلم بوجوب معرفة الله تعالى ، وأن النظر طريق إليها ولا طريق إليها سواه ، وأن مالا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب ، وكل واحد من هذه المقدمات نظرى والمرقوف على الظرى نظرى، فكان العلم بوجوب النظر عندهم نظريا، فللمخاطب أن يقول لا أنظار حتى لا أعرف وجوب النظر ، ثم الجواب أن الوجوب لا يتوقف على العلم بالوجوب وإلا لزم الدور بل يكفي فيه إمكان العلم بالوجوب ، والإمكان هنا حاصل في الجملة
أقول : حكى عن القفال الشاشى من أصحاب الشافعي ، وعن بعض الفقهاء الحنفية مع كونهم من أهل السنة أنهم قالوا بوجوب المعرفة عقلا ، والقول بأن المعرفة واجبة هى إحدى مقدمي المسألة المتقدمة وقوله فائدة الوجوب الثواب والعقاب فيه نظر ، لأن أهل السنة لا يوجبون الثواب على الطاعة ، والمعتزلة يقولون بأن القول بوجوب الثواب على الطاعة سمعى ، والوجوب العقلى يثبت باستحقاق تاركه الذم عقلا فهنا الاستدلال ساقط ، وأما قوله في معارضة المعتزلة فغير متوجه عليهم ، لأن وجوب النظر عندهم ليس بمتوقف على العلم بالوجوب، بل قالوا دفع الضرر المظنون الذى لعله يلحق بسبب الجهل بالمنعم واجب في البديهة العقلية وذلك لا يمكن إلا بمعرفته وذلك لا يزول بترك النظر بل إنما يزول بالنظر ، وأما الوجوب السمعى فلو كفى فيه إمكان العلم بالوجوب للزم أن يجب على المكلفين مالا يعلمونه أصلا ، لأن إمكان العلم بصدق الواجبات غير الذي يعلمونه حاصل، والصواب أن يقال : إمكان العلم بصدق الأوامر السمعية يقتضى وجوب النظر فيها
Issue: The obligation of rational reflection is called so, contrary to the Mu'tazila and some jurists from the Shafi'is and Hanafis. Our proof: His saying, Exalted: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger.' And because the benefit of obligation is reward and punishment, and nothing of His actions is ugly from God, so it is impossible to definitively assert reward and punishment through reason, so it is impossible to definitively assert obligation. They argued: that if obligation were only established by revelation whose truth is not known except through rational reflection, then the addressee could say: 'I will not reflect so that I do not know that the revelation is true,' and this leads to the nullification of the prophets. The answer: that this also applies to you, because the obligation of rational reflection, even if according to you it is rational, is not known by the necessity of reason. Rather, the knowledge of the obligation of reflection depends, according to the Mu'tazila, on the knowledge of the obligation to know God, and that reflection is the way to it and there is no other way to it, and that whatever without which an obligatory act is not completed is itself obligatory. Each of these premises is theoretical, and that which depends on the theoretical is theoretical. Thus the knowledge of the obligation of reflection according to them is theoretical. So the addressee could say: 'I will not reflect so that I do not know the obligation of reflection.' Then the answer: obligation does not depend on knowledge of obligation, otherwise circularity would result; rather, the possibility of knowledge of obligation suffices, and that possibility exists in general. I say: It is narrated from al-Qaffal al-Shashi among the Shafi'i scholars, and from some Hanafi jurists despite their being Ahl al-Sunna, that they affirmed the rational obligation of knowledge. The statement that knowledge is obligatory is one of the premises of the earlier issue, and his statement that the benefit of obligation is reward and punishment requires scrutiny, because the Ahl al-Sunna do not affirm obligation of reward for obedience, and the Mu'tazila say that the statement that reward for obedience is obligatory is scriptural. Rational obligation is established by the rational blameworthiness of abandoning it. So here the inference is invalid. As for his statement in refuting the Mu'tazila, it does not apply to them, because the obligation of reflection according to them does not depend on knowledge of obligation. Rather, they said: repelling probable harm that might be incurred due to ignorance of the Benefactor is obligatory by rational intuition, and that cannot be achieved except by knowledge of Him, and it is not removed by abandoning reflection but only by reflection. As for scriptural obligation, if possibility of knowledge of obligation sufficed for it, then it would be necessary that actions the doer does not know at all become obligatory, because the possibility of knowledge of the truth of scriptural commands other than what they know exists. The correct thing is to say: the possibility of knowledge of the truth of scriptural commands necessitates reflection upon them.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the Ash'ari position on the obligation of rational reflection (nazar) for attaining knowledge of God. It begins by stating the issue: the obligation of nazar is affirmed (سممى, likely 'called') in opposition to the Mu'tazila and some Shafi'i and Hanafi jurists. The Ash'ari proof is then given: God's verse 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' indicates that punishment depends on revelation, not reason. Furthermore, the benefit of obligation is reward and punishment, but nothing God does is morally ugly (qabih), so reason cannot definitively assert reward or punishment, and thus cannot definitively assert the obligation of nazar. The Mu'tazilite objection follows: if obligation is only established by revelation (sam'), whose truth is known only through nazar, then the addressee could say 'I will not reflect so I do not know revelation is true,' leading to a nullification of the prophets' mission. The Ash'ari answer is that this objection applies equally to the Mu'tazila themselves, because according to them, the obligation of nazar is rationally obligatory (wajib 'aqli), but it is not known immediately (darura). Rather, it depends on several theoretical premises: the obligation to know God, that nazar is the only way to that knowledge, and that whatever is necessary for an obligatory act is itself obligatory. All these premises are theoretical (nazari), so the knowledge of the obligation of nazar is also theoretical. Thus the same objection can be raised against them. The answer continues by explaining that obligation does not depend on actual knowledge of the obligation; otherwise circularity would result. It suffices that the possibility of knowing the obligation exists, and that possibility exists in general. The passage then includes a commentary (أقول) that discusses a report from al-Qaffal al-Shashi and some Hanafi jurists who affirmed rational obligation of knowledge. It critiques the earlier proof, noting that Ahl al-Sunna do not obligate reward, and Mu'tazila consider the obligation of reward as scriptural, not rational. The commentary concludes that the correct view is that the possibility of knowing the truth of scriptural commands necessitates reflection upon them.

Key Terms
وجوب النظر
السمع
العقل
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Ash'aris reject the obligation of nazar entirely.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm that nazar is obligatory, but only through revelation, not reason.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Ash'ari denial of rational obligation of nazar?
A) No verse commands reflection.
B) Reason cannot establish reward and punishment because nothing God does is ugly.
C) The Mu'tazila agree with them.
D) Obligation is only for acts of worship.

Key Takeaway
For the Ash'aris, the obligation of nazar is known only through revelation, not through rational deduction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral decision, do you instinctively rely on your own sense of right and wrong, or do you actively seek guidance from revelation? Razi's argument suggests that reason alone cannot ground obligation—has this ever been true in your own experience? Commit to checking one specific decision this week against scripture.
?
The Ash'aris use reason to argue for its own limits. Are you willing to do the same? In what area of your life are you most confident in your rationality, and how might that confidence be masking a need to submit to God's command? Write down one belief you hold that you have never critically examined in light of revelation.
?
If obligations are only truly known through revelation, how seriously do you take the revealed texts you have access to? Do you treat them as a source of binding commands or as advice? Ponder the weight of God's word and ask yourself: what is one command in the Quran or Sunnah that you have been neglecting, and why?

The Mu'tazilite Objection and the Ash'ari Response on Circularity
Razi presents the Mu'tazilite circularity objection and the Ash'ari response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objection that if obligation depends on revelation, which depends on nazar, then there is a circularity; and the Ash'ari response that obligation does not require actual knowledge of obligation, only its possibility.

Core Idea
Obligation does not depend on knowledge of the obligation; possibility of knowledge suffices.

The Mu'tazila objected that if the obligation of nazar is only known through revelation, then one must first verify revelation through nazar, leading to a vicious circle: nazar is needed to know that nazar is obligatory. Razi presents the Ash'ari answer, which also turns the objection against the Mu'tazila themselves, and then adds a more refined solution based on the possibility of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Notice the Mu'tazilite argument: without rational obligation, one can simply refuse to reflect, nullifying the prophets.
2
Read the Ash'ari response
Razi shows that the same circularity applies to the Mu'tazila because their own premises are theoretical.
3
Analyze the final answer
The key is that obligation does not depend on actually knowing the obligation, but only on the possibility of knowing it.
4
Consider the commentary
The commentary (أقول) discusses a related view and refines the argument.
This may be a later gloss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : وجوب النظر سممى خلافا للمتزلة وبعض الفقهاء من الشافعية والحنفية) لنا قوله تعالى : وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا ، ولأن فائدة الوجوب الثواب والعقاب ، ولا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء من أفعاله ، فلا يمكن القطع بالثواب والعقاب من جهة العقل ، فلا يمكن القطع بالوجوب .
واحتجوا : بأنه لو لم يثبت الوجوب إلا بالسمع الذى لا يعلم صحته إلا بالنظر فللمخاطب أن يقول : إنى لا أنظر حتى لا أعرف كون السمع صدقا وذلك مفض إلى إلام الأنبياء
والجواب : أن هذا لازم عليكم أيضاً لأن وجوب النظر وإن كان عندكم عقليا لكنه غير معلوم بضرورة العقل ، لا أن العلم بوجوب النظر يتوقف عند المعتزلة على العلم بوجوب معرفة الله تعالى ، وأن النظر طريق إليها ولا طريق إليها سواه ، وأن مالا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب ، وكل واحد من هذه المقدمات نظرى والمرقوف على الظرى نظرى، فكان العلم بوجوب النظر عندهم نظريا، فللمخاطب أن يقول لا أنظار حتى لا أعرف وجوب النظر ، ثم الجواب أن الوجوب لا يتوقف على العلم بالوجوب وإلا لزم الدور بل يكفي فيه إمكان العلم بالوجوب ، والإمكان هنا حاصل في الجملة
أقول : حكى عن القفال الشاشى من أصحاب الشافعي ، وعن بعض الفقهاء الحنفية مع كونهم من أهل السنة أنهم قالوا بوجوب المعرفة عقلا ، والقول بأن المعرفة واجبة هى إحدى مقدمي المسألة المتقدمة وقوله فائدة الوجوب الثواب والعقاب فيه نظر ، لأن أهل السنة لا يوجبون الثواب على الطاعة ، والمعتزلة يقولون بأن القول بوجوب الثواب على الطاعة سمعى ، والوجوب العقلى يثبت باستحقاق تاركه الذم عقلا فهنا الاستدلال ساقط ، وأما قوله في معارضة المعتزلة فغير متوجه عليهم ، لأن وجوب النظر عندهم ليس بمتوقف على العلم بالوجوب، بل قالوا دفع الضرر المظنون الذى لعله يلحق بسبب الجهل بالمنعم واجب في البديهة العقلية وذلك لا يمكن إلا بمعرفته وذلك لا يزول بترك النظر بل إنما يزول بالنظر ، وأما الوجوب السمعى فلو كفى فيه إمكان العلم بالوجوب للزم أن يجب على المكلفين مالا يعلمونه أصلا ، لأن إمكان العلم بصدق الواجبات غير الذي يعلمونه حاصل، والصواب أن يقال : إمكان العلم بصدق الأوامر السمعية يقتضى وجوب النظر فيها
Issue: The obligation of rational reflection is called so, contrary to the Mu'tazila and some jurists from the Shafi'is and Hanafis. Our proof: His saying, Exalted: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger.' And because the benefit of obligation is reward and punishment, and nothing of His actions is ugly from God, so it is impossible to definitively assert reward and punishment through reason, so it is impossible to definitively assert obligation. They argued: that if obligation were only established by revelation whose truth is not known except through rational reflection, then the addressee could say: 'I will not reflect so that I do not know that the revelation is true,' and this leads to the nullification of the prophets. The answer: that this also applies to you, because the obligation of rational reflection, even if according to you it is rational, is not known by the necessity of reason. Rather, the knowledge of the obligation of reflection depends, according to the Mu'tazila, on the knowledge of the obligation to know God, and that reflection is the way to it and there is no other way to it, and that whatever without which an obligatory act is not completed is itself obligatory. Each of these premises is theoretical, and that which depends on the theoretical is theoretical. Thus the knowledge of the obligation of reflection according to them is theoretical. So the addressee could say: 'I will not reflect so that I do not know the obligation of reflection.' Then the answer: obligation does not depend on knowledge of obligation, otherwise circularity would result; rather, the possibility of knowledge of obligation suffices, and that possibility exists in general. I say: It is narrated from al-Qaffal al-Shashi among the Shafi'i scholars, and from some Hanafi jurists despite their being Ahl al-Sunna, that they affirmed the rational obligation of knowledge. The statement that knowledge is obligatory is one of the premises of the earlier issue, and his statement that the benefit of obligation is reward and punishment requires scrutiny, because the Ahl al-Sunna do not affirm obligation of reward for obedience, and the Mu'tazila say that the statement that reward for obedience is obligatory is scriptural. Rational obligation is established by the rational blameworthiness of abandoning it. So here the inference is invalid. As for his statement in refuting the Mu'tazila, it does not apply to them, because the obligation of reflection according to them does not depend on knowledge of obligation. Rather, they said: repelling probable harm that might be incurred due to ignorance of the Benefactor is obligatory by rational intuition, and that cannot be achieved except by knowledge of Him, and it is not removed by abandoning reflection but only by reflection. As for scriptural obligation, if possibility of knowledge of obligation sufficed for it, then it would be necessary that actions the doer does not know at all become obligatory, because the possibility of knowledge of the truth of scriptural commands other than what they know exists. The correct thing is to say: the possibility of knowledge of the truth of scriptural commands necessitates reflection upon them.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the Ash'ari position on the obligation of rational reflection (nazar) for attaining knowledge of God. It begins by stating the issue: the obligation of nazar is affirmed (سممى, likely 'called') in opposition to the Mu'tazila and some Shafi'i and Hanafi jurists. The Mu'tazilite objection follows: if obligation is only established by revelation (sam'), whose truth is known only through nazar, then the addressee could say 'I will not reflect so I do not know revelation is true,' leading to a nullification of the prophets' mission. The Ash'ari answer is that this objection applies equally to the Mu'tazila themselves, because according to them, the obligation of nazar is rationally obligatory (wajib 'aqli), but it is not known immediately (darura). All these premises are theoretical (nazari), so the knowledge of the obligation of nazar is also theoretical. Thus the same objection can be raised against them. The answer continues by explaining that obligation does not depend on actual knowledge of the obligation; otherwise circularity would result. It suffices that the possibility of knowing the obligation exists, and that possibility exists in general. The passage then includes a commentary (أقول) that discusses a report from al-Qaffal al-Shashi and some Hanafi jurists who affirmed rational obligation of knowledge. It critiques the earlier proof, noting that Ahl al-Sunna do not obligate reward, and Mu'tazila consider the obligation of reward as scriptural, not rational. The commentary concludes that the correct view is that the possibility of knowing the truth of scriptural commands necessitates reflection upon them.

The First Obligatory Act: A Verbal Dispute
Razi lists opinions on the first obligatory act and explains the dispute is verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different opinions on the first obligatory act and why Razi considers the disagreement to be verbal rather than substantive.

Core Idea
The first obligatory act depends on defining 'first' and what is within human power.

In this passage, Razi tackles a foundational question: what is the very first act that Allah requires of a person? He presents three major scholarly opinions and then argues that the apparent disagreement is largely a matter of definition. By the end of this lesson, you will see how this issue hinges on how we define 'first' and whether knowledge is considered within human control.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opinions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three opinions: some say the first obligation is knowledge, some say it is reflection (nazar), and some say it is the intention to reflect.
2
Understand the verbal dispute analysis
Razi explains that the disagreement is verbal. He distinguishes between 'first' as the primary intended act and 'first' as the chronological first act. See how this shifts the answer depending on one's view of human power over knowledge and reflection.
3
Note the rejection of doubt
The commentary attributes to Imām al-Ḥaramayn the view that the first obligation is doubt. Razi rejects this because doubt is not within human power, and even if it were, a rational person would not desire it.
This shows that theological positions must align with what is rationally possible and desired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اختلفوا في أول الواجبات ) منهم من قال : هو المعرفة، ومنهم من قال : هو النظر المفيد المعرفة ، ومنهم من قال : هو القصد إلى هذا النظر وهذا خلاف لفظى ؛ لأنه إن كان المراد منه أول الواجبات المقصودة بالقصد الأول ، فلا شك أنه هو المعرفة عند من يجعلها مقدورة، والمنظر عند من لا يجعل العلم مقدورا ، وإن كان المراد أول الواجبات كيف كانت ، فلا شك أنه القصد (۲) ..
أقول : حكى عن أبي الحسن الأشعرى أن أول الواجبات هو العلم بالله تعالى، وأما القول بأن أول لواجبات هو النظر فهو مذهب المعتزلة ، وقيل إليه ذهب أبو إسحاق الإسفرائينى ، وذهب إمام الحرمين إلى أن أول الواجبات هو الشك وهذا ليس بصحيح ، لأن الشك لا يكون مقدورا ، وإن كان مقدورا فلا يكون مرادا للعاقل ، وسائر الاختلافات يتعلق باختلاف الاعتبارات كما بينه
Issue: They differed concerning the first of obligatory acts. Some said it is knowledge, some said it is reflection (nazar) that leads to knowledge, and some said it is the intention to this reflection. This is a verbal disagreement; because if what is meant is the first of obligatory acts intended as a primary intention, then undoubtedly it is knowledge according to those who consider it possible (maqdur), and reflection according to those who do not consider knowledge possible. And if what is meant is the first of obligatory acts however they are, then undoubtedly it is intention. I say: It is narrated from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari that the first of obligatory acts is knowledge of God, and as for the statement that the first of obligatory acts is reflection, it is the doctrine of the Mu'tazila, and it is said that Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini went to this. Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) went to the view that the first obligatory act is doubt, and this is not correct, because doubt is not possible (maqdur), and if it were possible, it would not be desired by a rational person. The rest of the differences relate to differences in considerations, as he explained.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage enumerates the different scholarly positions on the first obligatory act (awwal al-wajibat) in religion. Razi first states the issue: there is disagreement. Some say it is knowledge (ma'rifa), some say it is reflection (nazar) that produces knowledge, and some say it is the intention (qasd) to engage in that reflection. Razi immediately notes that this is a verbal dispute (khilaf lafzi). He gives the reason: if by 'first' we mean the primary intended obligatory act, then knowledge is first for those who consider knowledge an act within human power (maqdur), whereas reflection is first for those who do not consider knowledge to be directly within power (since reflection is the only way to it). If by 'first' we mean the very first act regardless of intention, then it is the intention (to reflect) that comes before the reflection itself. The passage then includes a commentary (أقول) that attributes to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari the view that the first obligation is knowledge of God, while the Mu'tazila and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held it is reflection. Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) held it is doubt (shakk), but Razi (through the commentator) rejects this because doubt is not within human power, and even if it were, it is not rationally desired. The commentary concludes that the remaining differences are due to differing perspectives (i'tibarat).

Key Terms
أول الواجبات
المعرفة
النظر
القصد
الخلاف اللفظي
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The different opinions on the first obligatory act are fundamentally incompatible and represent irreconcilable theological positions.
Correction: Razi shows that the dispute is verbal: the answer changes based on how 'first' is defined and whether knowledge is considered within human power. The positions can be reconciled once these definitions are clarified.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider the dispute over the first obligatory act to be verbal?
A) Because all scholars agree it is knowledge of God.
B) Because the answer changes depending on whether 'first' means primary intention or chronological first.
C) Because the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris have no real difference.
D) Because the first act is unknowable.

Key Takeaway
The first obligatory act is not a fixed answer; it depends on theological assumptions about human capacity and how 'first' is defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own journey to faith, was your first obligation knowledge of God or the search for it? Which have you truly prioritized in your life, and does that align with what you believe is within your power?
?
Razi dismisses doubt as not being within human power and not desired by a rational person. Yet many today valorize doubt as intellectual honesty. In your own heart, have you used doubt as an excuse to avoid seeking certainty, or have you treated it as a stepping stone to knowledge?
?
This lesson shows that even theological disputes can reduce to definitions and assumptions. Is there a belief or practice you hold as 'the most important' that might actually be shaped by your own unexamined assumptions about what is within your control? Are you willing to re-examine that today and change if needed?

Knowledge After Sound Reflection: Habit or Generation?
Razi presents the debate on how knowledge follows sound reflection and argues for necessity over generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions on how knowledge follows sound reflection, and why Razi favors necessity over generation.

Core Idea
Knowledge after reflection is necessary by divine habit, not by causal generation.

Razi introduces a key theological debate: does sound rational reflection (nazar) produce knowledge by habit (as Ash'aris hold) or by generation (as Mu'tazila claim)? He argues that knowledge necessarily follows reflection, but not as a causal effect. Instead, it is a divine practice. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two positions: habit (al-Ash'ari) and generation (al-Mu'tazila).
The text breaks off; focus on what is present.
2
Identify the two views
The Ash'ari view is that knowledge follows reflection by habit ('ada). The Mu'tazila view is that knowledge is generated (tawallud) by reflection itself.
Razi states the more correct view is necessity, not generation.
3
Examine Razi's example
Razi gives an example: knowing the world is changing and that everything changing is contingent. When these two items of knowledge are present, it is impossible not to know the world is contingent. This shows necessity.
The impossibility of not knowing is itself necessary knowledge.
4
Understand why Razi favors necessity
Razi argues that knowledge follows reflection necessarily (wujub), but not by generation. The example shows a logical necessity, not a causal one.
Generation implies causal power in the act itself, which is rejected.
5
Consider the missing argument
The text cuts off at 'وأما بطلان التولد'. Reflect on what arguments Razi might have used to invalidate generation, based on the Ash'ari rejection of secondary causality.
The incomplete text reminds us of the limits of this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : حصول العلم عقيب النظر الصحيح بالعادة عند الأشعرى وبالتولد عند المعتزلة ) والأصح الوجوب لا على سبيل التولد ، أما الوجوب ، فلان كل من علم أن العالم متغير وكل متغير ممكن قمع حضور هذين العلمين في الذهن يستحيل أن لا يعلم أن العالم ممكن، والعلم بهذا الامتناع ضرورى، وأما بطلان التولد
Issue: The attainment of knowledge after sound reflection is by habit ('ada) according to al-Ash'ari, and by generation (tawallud) according to the Mu'tazila. The more correct view is the necessity, not in the way of generation. As for the necessity: because everyone who knows that the world is changing and that everything changing is contingent, when these two items of knowledge are present in the mind, it is impossible that he does not know that the world is contingent. And knowledge of this impossibility is necessary. As for the invalidity of generation...
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents the debate on how knowledge is produced by sound rational reflection (nazar). According to al-Ash'ari, knowledge follows reflection by habit ('ada) – that is, God habitually creates knowledge in the mind after reflection, but not by causal necessity. The Mu'tazila hold that knowledge is generated (tawallud) by the act of reflection itself, as a causal consequence. Razi states that the more correct view (al-ashah) is the necessity (wujub) of knowledge following reflection, but not by way of generation. He gives an example: a person who knows that the world is changing and that everything changing is contingent – when these two pieces of knowledge are present in the mind, it is impossible for that person not to know that the world is contingent. The knowledge of this impossibility is itself necessary (daruri). Then he begins to argue for the invalidity of generation (bultan al-tawallud), but the text cuts off. The argument likely continues that generation is impossible because effects require efficient causation, which is reserved to God in Ash'ari thought.

Key Terms
حصول العلم
النظر الصحيح
العادة
التولد
الوجوب
الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ash'ari 'habit' means random or unpredictable, or that the necessity involved is a causal one like generation.
Correction: Ash'ari 'habit' is the regular divine custom, not randomness; the necessity here is logical – given premises, the conclusion follows necessarily – but the act of reflection does not cause the knowledge; God creates it.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position, how does knowledge follow from sound reflection?
A) By causal generation (tawallud) from the act of reflection.
B) By divine habit ('ada), meaning God creates knowledge following reflection.
C) By the necessity of thought, similar to generation.
D) It does not follow at all.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge follows sound reflection necessarily by God's habit, not by any causal power of the reflection itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that your reasoning leads you to truth. But do you rely on your own rational power, or do you recognize that any knowledge that comes after your reflection is ultimately a gift from God? Which view shapes your daily trust in your conclusions?
?
When you are certain of something you have reasoned through—like the existence of God or the contingency of the world—does that certainty make you humble or proud? How often do you thank Allah for creating that knowledge in your heart, rather than attributing it to your own intellect?
?
If knowledge after reflection is a divine habit and not a necessary effect, then every time you arrive at a truth, you are experiencing God’s custom. Does this awareness change how you approach learning and thinking? Will you now pause and acknowledge His role before you trust your reasoning?
