The First Obligation: A Verbal Dispute
Enumerates views on first obligation and resolves as verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the disagreement over the first religious obligation is a verbal dispute and how it depends on the meaning of 'first'.

Core Idea
The dispute over the first obligation is not real—it depends on what you mean by 'first'.

This passage tackles a classic theological disagreement: what is the very first thing a person is obliged to do? Is it knowledge, reflection, or intention? The author, Imam al-Razi, argues that this disagreement is merely verbal, not substantive. By clarifying the different senses of 'first', he shows how each opinion can be correct in its own perspective. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the central claim
Start by reading the first sentence where the author says the dispute is verbal. Notice how he defines the different senses of 'first'.
The phrase 'وهذا خلاف لفظى' is key.
2
Identify the three views
List the three positions: knowledge, reflection, intention. Understand each one briefly.
These correspond to different theological schools.
3
Analyze the resolution
See how the author resolves the dispute by distinguishing 'primary intention' from the general meaning of 'first'.
If 'first' means first intended, knowledge or reflection is first depending on one's view. If it means first in any sense, intention is first.
4
Examine the additional opinions
Read the commentary on al-Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, and Imam al-Haramayn. Note why the view that the first obligation is doubt is rejected.
Doubt is not an act within one's power that a rational person would intend.
5
Reflect on the lesson
The apparent disagreement collapses once we clarify what 'first' means. This shows the importance of precise definitions in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اختلفوا في أول الواجبات. منهم من قال: هو المعرفة، ومنهم من قال: هو النظر المفيد للمعرفة، ومنهم من قال: هو القصد إلى هذا النظر وهذا خلاف لفظى؛ لأنه إن كان المراد منه أول الواجبات المقصودة بالقصد الأول، فلا شك أنه هو المعرفة عند من يجعلها مقدورة، والنظر عند من لا يجعل العلم مقدورا، وإن كان المراد أول الواجبات كيف كانت، فلا شك أنه القصد.
(2) أقول : حكى عن أبي الحسن الأشعرى أن أول الواجبات هو العلم بالله تعالى، وأما القول بأن أول الواجبات هو النظر فهو مذهب المعتزلة، وقيل إليه ذهب أبو إسحاق الإسفرائينى، وذهب إمام الحرمين إلى أن أول الواجبات هو الشك وهذا ليس بصحيح، لأن الشك لا يكون مقدورا، وإن كان مقدورا فلا يكون مرادا للعاقل، وسائر الاختلافات يتعلق باختلاف الاعتبارات كما بينه.
Issue: They disagreed over the first of the obligations. Some said: it is knowledge, some said: it is reflection that leads to knowledge, and some said: it is the intention to this reflection. And this is a verbal dispute; because if what is meant by it is the first of the obligations that are intended by primary intention, then there is no doubt that it is knowledge according to those who make it attainable, and reflection according to those who do not make knowledge attainable. And if what is meant is the first obligation however it may be, then there is no doubt that it is intention.
(2) I say: It is related from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari that the first of the obligations is knowledge of God Almighty. As for the statement that the first obligation is reflection, it is the doctrine of the Mu'tazila, and it is said that Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini went to it. And Imam al-Haramayn went to the view that the first obligation is doubt, and this is not correct, because doubt is not attainable, and if it were attainable, it would not be intended by a rational person. And the rest of the disagreements relate to differences in considerations, as he explained.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage classifies the various opinions on the first obligation. It first lists three views: knowledge (ma'rifah), reflection (nazar) that yields knowledge, and intention (qasd) to that reflection. It then argues that this is a verbal dispute, because if 'first' means the first intended obligation, then the answer depends on whether one considers knowledge achievable directly; if not, reflection is first. If 'first' means the first in any sense, then intention is clearly first. The commentary adds that al-Ash'ari held the first obligation to be knowledge, the Mu'tazila held it to be reflection, Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini reportedly also held reflection, and Imam al-Haramayn held it to be doubt, which is rejected because doubt is not an act within one's power that a rational person would intend. The disagreements are ultimately about different considerations.

Key Terms
أول الواجبات
المعرفة
النظر
القصد
الخلاف اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The different views on the first obligation represent real, incompatible theological positions.
Correction: The author shows that the disagreement is merely verbal, depending on what is meant by 'first'.

Checkpoint
What is the author's conclusion about the disagreements concerning the first obligation?
A) They are real and irreconcilable
B) They are a verbal dispute depending on what is meant by 'first'
C) Knowledge is definitely the first obligation
D) Intention is always the first obligation

Key Takeaway
The apparent disagreement over the first obligation is resolved by distinguishing different meanings of 'first', showing the importance of clarifying terms in theological debates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a disagreement about religious obligations, do you first examine whether the disagreement is about definitions or about substance? How often do you assume you understand what the other person means by key terms like 'knowledge' or 'reflection'?
?
If the first obligation in any sense is intention (qasd), what does that mean for how you approach your daily actions? Are you mindful of your intentions before you even begin an act of worship or learning? Does this shift your focus from mere actions to the heart's purpose?
?
The author dismisses doubt as a possible first obligation because it is not within one's power and a rational person would not intend it. Reflect: Are there areas of your faith where you allow doubt to linger, or even consider it a starting point? Do you see doubt as an obstacle or as something that needs to be resolved through reflection?

The Three Positions on Knowledge After Reflection
Classification of three positions and proof for necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on how knowledge occurs after correct reflection (nazar) and the proof that it is a necessary consequence.

Core Idea
Knowledge after reflection is necessary, but not generated by the reflector.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi lays out the three main positions on the nature of knowledge that follows correct reflection. He begins with the Ash'ari view that it is purely habitual from God and the Mu'tazili view that it is generated by the reflector. Then he states his own synthesis: it is necessary (wajib) but not generated, and he gives a logical proof for its necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of positions
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the author states the Ash'ari (habit) and Mu'tazili (generation) views, and then his own preferred view of necessity without generation.
Notice that the author does not fully reject either view but synthesizes them.
2
Understand the proof for necessity
Follow the logical proof: if one knows two premises (the world is changing and everything changing is possible), then the conclusion (the world is possible) necessarily follows. The impossibility of not knowing this conclusion is self-evident.
This proof shows that knowledge after nazar is a necessary concomitant of the premises.
3
Reflect on the author's middle position
Consider how the author's position differs from both Ash'ari and Mu'tazila: he agrees with Ash'ari that knowledge is from God, but disagrees that it could fail to occur; he agrees with Mu'tazila that it is necessary, but disagrees that it is generated by the creature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: حصول العلم عقيب النظر الصحيح بالعادة عند الأشعرى وبالتولد عند المعتزلة
والأصح الوجوب لا على سبيل التولد، أما الوجوب، فلان كل من علم أن العالم متغير وكل متغير ممكن فعند حضور هذين العلمين في الذهن يستحيل أن لا يعلم أن العالم ممكن، والعلم بهذا الامتناع ضرورى، وأما بطلان التولد فلأن العلم فى نفسه ممكن فيكون مقدورا الله تعالى فيمتنع وقوعه بغير قدرته، والقياس على التذكر لا يفيد اليقين ولا الإلزام، لأنهم إنما لم يقولوا فى التذكر علة لا توجد في النظر، فإن صحت تلك العلة ظهر الفرق وإلا منعوا الحكم في الأصل (1)
(1) أقول : الأشعرى يقول لا مؤثر إلا الله تعالى، والعلم بعد النظر حادث محتاج إلى المؤثر فاذا هو فعل الله تعالى، وليس على الله شيء واجبا وقوعه غير واجب وهو أكثرى فهو عادى كطلوع الشمس كل يوم. وأن أفعال الله المكررة يقال إنه جعلها بإجراء العادة، وكل ما لا يتكرر أو يتكرر قليلا فهو خارق للعادة أو نادر
وأما المعتزلة فلما أثبتوا لبعض الحوادث مؤثرا غير الله تعالى قالوا بأن كل فعل يصدر عن فاعله بلا توسط شيء آخر كالاعتماد من الحيوان، يقولون إنه حصل منه بالمباشرة وكل ما يصدر عنه بتوسط شيء آخر كالحركة التي تصدر عنه بواسطة الاعتماد يقولون إنه حصل منه بالتوليد .
وههنا قال الأشعرى: إن الله يخلق العلم بعد النظر على سبيل إجراء العادة وليس بممتنع أن لا يخلقه بعده . وقال المعتزلى : إنه حصل من الناظر بتوسط النظر على سبيل التوليد فهو متولد واجب وقوعه بعد النظر وقوع المعلول بعد العلة التامة
وصاحب الكتاب وافق الأشعرى فى كونه من فعل الله تعالى ووافق المعتزلى في كونه واجب الوقوع بعد النظر، وخالف الأشعرى فى قوله ليس بممتنع أن لا يخلقه، وخالف المعتزلى في أنه من فعل الناظر واستدل على الوجوب بالمثال الذي ذكره، وله أن يدعى ذلك في جميع اللوازم مع الملزومات .
و للأشعرى أن يمنع قوله فمع حضور هذين العلمين في الذهن يستحيل أن لا يحصل العلم بالنتيجة بخوارق العادات، فإن العاقل يحكم باستحالة وقوع النطق من الجمادات، وقد يقع ذلك عند ظهور المعجز من الأنبياء . قيل وإنما أخذ صاحب الكتاب هذا القول من القاضى أبى بكر الباقلانى وإمام الحرمين فإنهما قالا باستلزام النظر العلم على سبيل الوجوب لا يكون النظر علة أو مولدا.
ثم إن الاشعرية ردوا قول المعتزلة باستعمال القياس، فإن القدماء من المتكلمين كانوا يستعملون القياس في علم الأصول الذى يستعمل في الفقه وهو الحاق فرع بالأصل في حكم بسبب جامع لهما يدعون أنه السبب في الحكم بالأصل وهو موجود فى الفرع، فيجب أن يكون سببه وهو الحكم موجوداً أيضاً في الفرع . وطلاب اليقين لا يعتمدون عليه بل يقولون : هذا القياس على تقدير صحته لا يفيد اليقين بل يوقع ظنا فقط وعلى تقدير المساعدة في استعماله ربما يفرقون بين الأصل والفرع بما يمنع كون الجامع مقتضيا للحكم فى الفرع وإن كان مقتضيا له في الأصل . فقال المصنف : قياس الأشعرى النظرى فى قوله إن النظر لا يولد العلم على التذكر فإن المعتزلى يوافقه في أن التذكر لا يولد العلم الذي يعود بالتذكر لا يفيد اليقين لكونه قياسا غير مفيد لليقين ولا إلزام على تقدير المساعدة في استعماله القياس فى المطالب العقلية، لأن المعتزلة لم يقولوا بالتولد في التذكر، لعلة توجد في التذكر ولا توجد في النظر وتلك العلة أن التذكر ربما يحصل من غير قصد المتذكر، والنظر لا يعمل من غير قصد الناظر، فإن صحت تلك العلة ظهر الفرق فسقط الاستدلال بهذا القياس، وإلا منع ←
Issue: The occurrence of knowledge after correct reflection: according to al-Ash'ari it is by habit (al-'adah), and according to the Mu'tazila it is by generation (al-tawallud).
The more correct view is obligation, not by way of generation. As for the obligation, because whoever knows that the world is changing and every changing thing is possible, then at the presence of these two knowledges in the mind it is impossible that he does not know that the world is possible, and the knowledge of this impossibility is necessary. As for the invalidity of generation, because knowledge in itself is possible, so it is within the power of God Almighty, so its occurrence without His power is impossible. And the analogy (al-qiyas) to remembrance (al-tadhakkur) does not yield certainty nor necessity, because they only did not say in remembrance a cause that does not exist in reflection; if that cause is sound, the difference appears, otherwise they prevent the ruling in the original. (1)
(1) I say: Al-Ash'ari says there is no effect except God Almighty, and knowledge after reflection is an accident needing an effect, so it is the act of God Almighty, and nothing is obligatory upon God, its occurrence is not obligatory, and it is habitual (most often), so it is customary like the rising of the sun every day. And the repeated acts of God are said to be made by Him by way of habit, and whatever does not repeat or repeats rarely is miraculous or rare.
As for the Mu'tazila, since they established an effect other than God for some accidents, they said that every action that issues from its agent without the mediation of anything else, like reliance from an animal, they say it occurs from it directly; and whatever issues from it through the mediation of something else, like motion that issues from it through reliance, they say it occurs from it by generation.
And here al-Ash'ari said: God creates knowledge after reflection by way of habit, and it is not impossible that He does not create it after it. And the Mu'tazili said: It occurs from the reflector through the mediation of reflection by way of generation, so it is generated, its occurrence after reflection is necessary as the occurrence of the effect after the complete cause.
And the author of the book agreed with al-Ash'ari in it being from the act of God Almighty, and agreed with the Mu'tazila in it being necessarily occurring after reflection, and disagreed with al-Ash'ari in his statement that it is not impossible that He does not create it, and disagreed with the Mu'tazila in that it is from the act of the reflector, and he argued for the necessity with the example he mentioned, and he can claim that in all concomitants with their necessary conditions.
And al-Ash'ari can prevent his statement: 'At the presence of these two knowledges in the mind it is impossible that knowledge of the result does not occur' by miracles that break habits, for the rational person judges the impossibility of speech occurring from inanimate objects, and that may occur at the appearance of miracles from the prophets. It is said that the author of the book took this statement from al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn, for they both said that reflection necessitates knowledge by way of obligation, not that reflection is a cause or generator.
Then the Ash'aris refuted the statement of the Mu'tazila by using analogy (al-qiyas), for the early theologians used to use analogy in the science of principles (usul) which is used in jurisprudence, which is attaching a branch to the root in a ruling due to a commonality that they claim is the cause of the ruling in the root and exists in the branch, so its effect, namely the ruling, must also exist in the branch. And seekers of certainty do not rely on it, but say: This analogy, assuming its validity, does not yield certainty but only yields probability, and assuming cooperation in its use, perhaps they differentiate between the root and the branch by something that prevents the commonality from being effective for the ruling in the branch even if it is effective in the root. So the author said: The analogy of al-Ash'ari regarding his statement that reflection does not generate knowledge, based on remembrance, for the Mu'tazili agrees with him that remembrance does not generate knowledge that returns through remembrance, does not yield certainty because it is an analogy that does not yield certainty nor is it binding, assuming cooperation in using analogy in intellectual matters, because the Mu'tazila did not say generation in remembrance, due to a cause that exists in remembrance and does not exist in reflection, and that cause is that remembrance perhaps occurs without the intention of the one remembering, while reflection does not work without the intention of the reflector. If that cause is sound, the difference appears, so the inference by this analogy falls; otherwise, they prevent...
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part discussion about the nature of knowledge that follows correct reflection (al-nazar). It begins with a classification: al-Ash'ari holds it occurs by divine habit (adah), while the Mu'tazila hold it is generated (mutawallad) by the reflector. The author (Razi) states the more correct view is that it is necessary (wajib) but not by generation. He proves the necessity: anyone who knows that the world is changing and that every changing thing is possible cannot help but know that the world is possible; this is a necessary implication. He then refutes generation: knowledge is possible, so it must be within God's power, thus cannot occur by the power of a creature. He dismisses the Ash'ari's analogy to remembrance (tadhakkur) as lacking certainty and binding force. The commentary then elaborates: al-Ash'ari's occasionalism, the Mu'tazili theory of direct and generated acts, and the author's middle position: knowledge after nazar is necessarily from God, not generated by the reflector, but it is not merely habitual; it is a necessary concomitant. The Ash'ari object that miracles could break the habit, but the author's proof does not rely on habit. Finally, the Ash'ari use of qiyas is refuted: the analogy to remembrance fails because remembrance can occur without intention, while nazar cannot; hence the common cause is absent.

Key Terms
العلم
النظر
العادة
التولد
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author's position is identical to either the Ash'ari or Mu'tazili position.
Correction: The author synthesizes both: knowledge is from God (as Ash'ari says) but necessary (as Mu'tazila claim), and he rejects both the Ash'ari claim that it could fail and the Mu'tazili claim that it is generated by the reflector.

Checkpoint
What is the author's (Razi's) position on the occurrence of knowledge after correct reflection?
A) It occurs by habit (adah) as al-Ash'ari says
B) It occurs by generation (tawallud) as the Mu'tazila say
C) It is necessary (wajib) but not by generation, and from God
D) It is neither necessary nor generated, but purely voluntary

Key Takeaway
Knowledge after correct reflection is a necessary concomitant of the premises, not a generated effect of the reflector.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reach a conclusion through your own reasoning, do you tend to feel that you have 'generated' that truth yourself, or do you recognize it as something God has created in you? How does this lesson challenge your pride in intellect?
?
Is there a truth that you know clearly from evidence (like the impossibility of denying a logical conclusion) but you still resist because of your desires? Will you commit today to submit to the truth even when it is uncomfortable?
?
The Ash'ari view says knowledge after reflection is just a habit, not guaranteed—when was the last time you presumed that your own reasoning would automatically lead to truth without relying on God? Will you repent of that self-reliance?

Refuting Generation and the Analogy to Remembrance
Refutation of generation and dismissal of analogy to remembrance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili theory of generation (tawallud) is invalid and why the Ash'ari analogy to remembrance (tadhakkur) is not convincing.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot be generated by a creature, and analogies to remembrance lack certainty.

After establishing the necessity of knowledge after reflection, Razi now refutes the two alternative accounts: the Mu'tazili claim that knowledge is generated by the reflector, and the Ash'ari argument that it is merely habitual and analogous to remembrance. He shows that generation is impossible because knowledge is within God's power alone, and the analogy fails because the two cases are not comparable.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of generation
Read the first part of the passage where the author argues against generation: knowledge is possible, so it must be within God's power; therefore its occurrence without God's power is impossible.
Note the underlying premise: everything possible is either created by God or generated by a creature, but generation is not valid for knowledge.
2
Read the dismissal of the analogy
The author then addresses the Ash'ari analogy that knowledge after nazar is like remembering something after reflection. He says this analogy does not yield certainty nor binding force because there is a relevant difference.
Look for the reason why the analogy fails: remembrance can occur without intention, but nazar requires intention.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author argues that if there is a genuine cause (illah) that makes remembrance not generated, and that cause is absent in nazar, then the analogy fails. If the cause is not sound, then the original ruling (that remembrance does not generate knowledge) is not accepted.
This shows the weakness of using qiyas (analogy) without a shared effective cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: حصول العلم عقيب النظر الصحيح بالعادة عند الأشعرى وبالتولد عند المعتزلة
والأصح الوجوب لا على سبيل التولد، أما الوجوب، فلان كل من علم أن العالم متغير وكل متغير ممكن فعند حضور هذين العلمين في الذهن يستحيل أن لا يعلم أن العالم ممكن، والعلم بهذا الامتناع ضرورى، وأما بطلان التولد فلأن العلم فى نفسه ممكن فيكون مقدورا الله تعالى فيمتنع وقوعه بغير قدرته، والقياس على التذكر لا يفيد اليقين ولا الإلزام، لأنهم إنما لم يقولوا فى التذكر علة لا توجد في النظر، فإن صحت تلك العلة ظهر الفرق وإلا منعوا الحكم في الأصل (1)
(1) أقول : الأشعرى يقول لا مؤثر إلا الله تعالى، والعلم بعد النظر حادث محتاج إلى المؤثر فاذا هو فعل الله تعالى، وليس على الله شيء واجبا وقوعه غير واجب وهو أكثرى فهو عادى كطلوع الشمس كل يوم. وأن أفعال الله المكررة يقال إنه جعلها بإجراء العادة، وكل ما لا يتكرر أو يتكرر قليلا فهو خارق للعادة أو نادر
وأما المعتزلة فلما أثبتوا لبعض الحوادث مؤثرا غير الله تعالى قالوا بأن كل فعل يصدر عن فاعله بلا توسط شيء آخر كالاعتماد من الحيوان، يقولون إنه حصل منه بالمباشرة وكل ما يصدر عنه بتوسط شيء آخر كالحركة التي تصدر عنه بواسطة الاعتماد يقولون إنه حصل منه بالتوليد .
وههنا قال الأشعرى: إن الله يخلق العلم بعد النظر على سبيل إجراء العادة وليس بممتنع أن لا يخلقه بعده . وقال المعتزلى : إنه حصل من الناظر بتوسط النظر على سبيل التوليد فهو متولد واجب وقوعه بعد النظر وقوع المعلول بعد العلة التامة
وصاحب الكتاب وافق الأشعرى فى كونه من فعل الله تعالى ووافق المعتزلى في كونه واجب الوقوع بعد النظر، وخالف الأشعرى فى قوله ليس بممتنع أن لا يخلقه، وخالف المعتزلى في أنه من فعل الناظر واستدل على الوجوب بالمثال الذي ذكره، وله أن يدعى ذلك في جميع اللوازم مع الملزومات .
و للأشعرى أن يمنع قوله فمع حضور هذين العلمين في الذهن يستحيل أن لا يحصل العلم بالنتيجة بخوارق العادات، فإن العاقل يحكم باستحالة وقوع النطق من الجمادات، وقد يقع ذلك عند ظهور المعجز من الأنبياء . قيل وإنما أخذ صاحب الكتاب هذا القول من القاضى أبى بكر الباقلانى وإمام الحرمين فإنهما قالا باستلزام النظر العلم على سبيل الوجوب لا يكون النظر علة أو مولدا.
ثم إن الاشعرية ردوا قول المعتزلة باستعمال القياس، فإن القدماء من المتكلمين كانوا يستعملون القياس في علم الأصول الذى يستعمل في الفقه وهو الحاق فرع بالأصل في حكم بسبب جامع لهما يدعون أنه السبب في الحكم بالأصل وهو موجود فى الفرع، فيجب أن يكون سببه وهو الحكم موجوداً أيضاً في الفرع . وطلاب اليقين لا يعتمدون عليه بل يقولون : هذا القياس على تقدير صحته لا يفيد اليقين بل يوقع ظنا فقط وعلى تقدير المساعدة في استعماله ربما يفرقون بين الأصل والفرع بما يمنع كون الجامع مقتضيا للحكم فى الفرع وإن كان مقتضيا له في الأصل . فقال المصنف : قياس الأشعرى النظرى فى قوله إن النظر لا يولد العلم على التذكر فإن المعتزلى يوافقه في أن التذكر لا يولد العلم الذي يعود بالتذكر لا يفيد اليقين لكونه قياسا غير مفيد لليقين ولا إلزام على تقدير المساعدة في استعماله القياس فى المطالب العقلية، لأن المعتزلة لم يقولوا بالتولد في التذكر، لعلة توجد في التذكر ولا توجد في النظر وتلك العلة أن التذكر ربما يحصل من غير قصد المتذكر، والنظر لا يعمل من غير قصد الناظر، فإن صحت تلك العلة ظهر الفرق فسقط الاستدلال بهذا القياس، وإلا منع ←
Issue: The occurrence of knowledge after correct reflection: according to al-Ash'ari it is by habit (al-'adah), and according to the Mu'tazila it is by generation (al-tawallud).
The more correct view is obligation, not by way of generation. As for the obligation, because whoever knows that the world is changing and every changing thing is possible, then at the presence of these two knowledges in the mind it is impossible that he does not know that the world is possible, and the knowledge of this impossibility is necessary. As for the invalidity of generation, because knowledge in itself is possible, so it is within the power of God Almighty, so its occurrence without His power is impossible. And the analogy (al-qiyas) to remembrance (al-tadhakkur) does not yield certainty nor necessity, because they only did not say in remembrance a cause that does not exist in reflection; if that cause is sound, the difference appears, otherwise they prevent the ruling in the original. (1)
(1) I say: Al-Ash'ari says there is no effect except God Almighty, and knowledge after reflection is an accident needing an effect, so it is the act of God Almighty, and nothing is obligatory upon God, its occurrence is not obligatory, and it is habitual (most often), so it is customary like the rising of the sun every day. And the repeated acts of God are said to be made by Him by way of habit, and whatever does not repeat or repeats rarely is miraculous or rare.
As for the Mu'tazila, since they established an effect other than God for some accidents, they said that every action that issues from its agent without the mediation of anything else, like reliance from an animal, they say it occurs from it directly; and whatever issues from it through the mediation of something else, like motion that issues from it through reliance, they say it occurs from it by generation.
And here al-Ash'ari said: God creates knowledge after reflection by way of habit, and it is not impossible that He does not create it after it. And the Mu'tazili said: It occurs from the reflector through the mediation of reflection by way of generation, so it is generated, its occurrence after reflection is necessary as the occurrence of the effect after the complete cause.
And the author of the book agreed with al-Ash'ari in it being from the act of God Almighty, and agreed with the Mu'tazila in it being necessarily occurring after reflection, and disagreed with al-Ash'ari in his statement that it is not impossible that He does not create it, and disagreed with the Mu'tazila in that it is from the act of the reflector, and he argued for the necessity with the example he mentioned, and he can claim that in all concomitants with their necessary conditions.
And al-Ash'ari can prevent his statement: 'At the presence of these two knowledges in the mind it is impossible that knowledge of the result does not occur' by miracles that break habits, for the rational person judges the impossibility of speech occurring from inanimate objects, and that may occur at the appearance of miracles from the prophets. It is said that the author of the book took this statement from al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn, for they both said that reflection necessitates knowledge by way of obligation, not that reflection is a cause or generator.
Then the Ash'aris refuted the statement of the Mu'tazila by using analogy (al-qiyas), for the early theologians used to use analogy in the science of principles (usul) which is used in jurisprudence, which is attaching a branch to the root in a ruling due to a commonality that they claim is the cause of the ruling in the root and exists in the branch, so its effect, namely the ruling, must also exist in the branch. And seekers of certainty do not rely on it, but say: This analogy, assuming its validity, does not yield certainty but only yields probability, and assuming cooperation in its use, perhaps they differentiate between the root and the branch by something that prevents the commonality from being effective for the ruling in the branch even if it is effective in the root. So the author said: The analogy of al-Ash'ari regarding his statement that reflection does not generate knowledge, based on remembrance, for the Mu'tazili agrees with him that remembrance does not generate knowledge that returns through remembrance, does not yield certainty because it is an analogy that does not yield certainty nor is it binding, assuming cooperation in using analogy in intellectual matters, because the Mu'tazila did not say generation in remembrance, due to a cause that exists in remembrance and does not exist in reflection, and that cause is that remembrance perhaps occurs without the intention of the one remembering, while reflection does not work without the intention of the reflector. If that cause is sound, the difference appears, so the inference by this analogy falls; otherwise, they prevent...
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part discussion about the nature of knowledge that follows correct reflection (al-nazar). It begins with a classification: al-Ash'ari holds it occurs by divine habit (adah), while the Mu'tazila hold it is generated (mutawallad) by the reflector. The author (Razi) states the more correct view is that it is necessary (wajib) but not by generation. He proves the necessity: anyone who knows that the world is changing and that every changing thing is possible cannot help but know that the world is possible; this is a necessary implication. He then refutes generation: knowledge is possible, so it must be within God's power, thus cannot occur by the power of a creature. He dismisses the Ash'ari's analogy to remembrance (tadhakkur) as lacking certainty and binding force. The commentary then elaborates: al-Ash'ari's occasionalism, the Mu'tazili theory of direct and generated acts, and the author's middle position: knowledge after nazar is necessarily from God, not generated by the reflector, but it is not merely habitual; it is a necessary concomitant. The Ash'ari object that miracles could break the habit, but the author's proof does not rely on habit. Finally, the Ash'ari use of qiyas is refuted: the analogy to remembrance fails because remembrance can occur without intention, while nazar cannot; hence the common cause is absent.

Key Terms
التولد
القياس
التذكر
الوجوب
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That generation (tawallud) is a valid theory for the occurrence of knowledge.
Correction: Razi argues that knowledge is possible and thus must be created by God alone; it cannot be generated by a creature's act.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that knowledge cannot be generated (mutawallad)?
A) Because knowledge is eternal
B) Because knowledge is possible and thus must be within God's power alone
C) Because knowledge is a physical accident
D) Because the reflector is not a real agent

Key Takeaway
Generated knowledge is impossible because only God creates knowledge, and analogical arguments that ignore essential differences are not probative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel you have 'discovered' a truth through your own thinking, do you acknowledge that God alone is the creator of that knowledge in your heart? How would your learning change if you constantly remembered this?
?
Think of a belief you hold about God or religion that you have built on an analogy from everyday experience. Has this passage exposed a hidden flaw in your reasoning? Will you now seek a more certain foundation?
?
Can you recall a time when you dismissed someone's argument by pointing out a difference in context, but later realized you were inconsistent in your own use of analogies? What does this lesson demand of you in how you evaluate arguments?

The Two Views on Knowledge After Reflection
Razi presents background and two opposing views on knowledge after nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite positions on the causation of knowledge after reflection and their underlying assumptions.

Core Idea
Knowledge after reflection: a divine habit or a generated effect.

Razi opens the discussion by establishing that knowledge is possible and must be within God's power. He then presents the two main theological positions: the Ash'ari view that God creates knowledge after reflection by habit, and the Mu'tazili view that it is generated by the reflector's action. Understanding these positions is crucial for the reconciliation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical flow: possibility of knowledge, dismissal of analogy, then Mu'tazila's theory.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the Ash'ari view (knowledge created by God after reflection by way of habit) and the Mu'tazili view (knowledge generated by the reflector through reflection).
3
Understand the Mu'tazila's theory of generation
The Mu'tazila distinguish between direct actions (bi al-mubashara) and generated actions (bi al-tawleed). Reflection is a direct action, knowledge is generated.
This distinction is key to their position.
4
Examine the dismissal of the recollection analogy
The analogy to recollection is rejected because the cause is different. Reflect on why this analogy fails to provide certainty.
This shows the argument is not analogical but based on first principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلأن العلم فى نفسه ممكن فيكون مقدورا الله تعالى فيمتنع وقوعه بغير قدرته ، والقياس على التذكر لا يفيد اليقين ولا الإلزام ، لأنهم إنما لم يقولوا فى التذكر املة لا توجد في النظر ، فإن صحت تلك الملة ظهر الفرق وإلا منعوا الحكم في الأصل . وأما المعتزلة فلما أثبتوا لبعض الحوادث مؤثرا غير الله تعالى قالوا بأن كل فعل يصدر عن فاعله بلا توسط شیء آخر كالاعتماد من الحيوان، يقولون إنه حصل منه بالمباشرة وكل ما يصدر عنه بتوسط شيء آخر كالحركة التي تصدر عنه بواسطة الاعتماد يقولون إنه حصل منه بالتوليد . وههنا قال الأشعرى: إن الله يخلق العلم بعد النظر على سبيل إجراء العادة وليس بممتنع أن لا يخلقه بعده . وقال المعتزلى : إنه عصل من الناظر بتوسط النظر على سبيل التوليد فهو متولد واجب وقوعه بعد النظر وقوع المعلول بعد العلة التامة . وصاحب الكتاب : وافق الأشعرى فى كونه من فعل الله تعالى ووافق المعتزلى في كونه واجب الوقوع بعد النظر ، وخالف الأشعرى فى قوله ليس بممتنع أن لا يخلقه ، وخالف المعتزلى في أنه من فعل الناظر واستدل على الوجوب بالمثال الذي ذكره ، وله أن يدعى ذلك في جميع اللوازم مع الملزومات .
Because knowledge in itself is possible, so it is within the power of God, and its occurrence without His power is impossible. The analogy to recollection does not provide certainty nor binding proof, because they only did not say in recollection a cause that does not exist in reflection; if that cause is sound, the difference appears, otherwise they would prevent the ruling in the original. As for the Mu'tazila, since they affirmed for some events an efficient cause other than God, they said that every action proceeds from its agent without mediation of anything else, like reliance from an animal; they say it occurs from him directly, and everything that proceeds from him through mediation of another thing, like movement that proceeds from him through reliance, they say it occurs from him by generation. And here the Ash'ari said: God creates knowledge after reflection by way of habit, and it is not impossible that He does not create it afterwards. And the Mu'tazili said: It occurs from the reflector through mediation of reflection by way of generation, so it is generated, and its occurrence after reflection is necessary like the occurrence of the effect after the complete cause. And the author of the book: agreed with the Ash'ari in that it is from God, and agreed with the Mu'tazila in that it is necessary in occurrence after reflection, and disagreed with the Ash'ari in his statement that it is not impossible that He does not create it, and disagreed with the Mu'tazila in that it is from the reflector, and he argued for necessity by the example he mentioned, and he can claim that for all concomitants with their objects.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents the views of the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila on whether knowledge after reflection is from God or generated by the reflector, and then states the author's reconciliatory position. The argument begins by establishing that knowledge is possible and therefore must be within God's power; it cannot occur without His power. A comparison with recollection is made but dismissed as not yielding certainty. Then the Mu'tazila's theory of direct and generated actions is explained. The Ash'ari holds that God creates knowledge after reflection by habit, and it is not impossible that He does not. The Mu'tazili holds that knowledge is generated by the reflector and necessarily occurs. The author agrees with the Ash'ari that it is from God and with the Mu'tazila that it is necessary, but disagrees with the Ash'ari on possibility of non-occurrence and with the Mu'tazila on the agent. He argues for necessity and claims his reasoning can be applied to all concomitants.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, how does knowledge after reflection occur?
A) It is created directly by God without any intermediary.
B) It is generated by the reflector through the mediation of reflection.
C) It is produced by the reflector directly without any mediation.
D) It occurs purely by habit and is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari and Mu'tazila differ fundamentally on whether knowledge after reflection is a divine habit or a generated effect of the reflector.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you acquire new understanding in your faith or studies, do you genuinely feel that it is a direct gift from God, or do you sense a pride in your own intellect? What would change if you truly internalized that every piece of knowledge is entirely dependent on God's power?
?
The Mu'tazila attribute generated acts to the creature—do you, in your daily life, attribute your successes, achievements, or even good deeds to your own ability, forgetting that even the capacity to act is sustained by God? How can you practice attributing everything back to Him?
?
The Ash'ari view insists that knowledge after reflection is a divine habit, not a necessary effect. Are you comfortable with the uncertainty that your next insight or understanding is not guaranteed by your effort but only by God's continuous will? Does this thought humble you or disturb you?

The Author's Reconciliation: Knowledge from God, Necessarily After Reflection
Al-Razi presents the author's reconciliation of the two schools on knowledge after nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's reconciliatory position that knowledge after reflection is created by God but necessarily follows reflection, and his argument for this necessity.

Core Idea
The author synthesizes: knowledge is from God but necessarily occurs after reflection.

After presenting the two rival positions, the author of the book offers his own view. He agrees with the Ash'ari that knowledge is created by God, but he also agrees with the Mu'tazila that knowledge necessarily occurs after reflection—a necessity he defends with an example and extends to all concomitants.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the three positions
Read the Arabic passage that presents the Ash'ari view, the Mu'tazili view, and the author's view.
Notice the four points of agreement/disagreement.
2
Analyze the author's synthesis
The author agrees with Ash'ari on the agent (God) and with Mu'tazila on necessity. He disagrees with Ash'ari on the possibility of non-occurrence and with Mu'tazila on the human agent.
This is a classic reconciliation in kalam.
3
Understand the argument for necessity
The author argues for necessity by an example (mentioned earlier in the text). He claims the same relationship holds for all concomitants with their objects.
This argument is based on the logical relationship between a concomitant (lazim) and its object (malzum).
4
Reflect on the extension to all concomitants
The author claims his reasoning applies universally: whenever A necessitates B, then given A, B must occur. This aligns with Mu'tazilite causal necessity but under divine agency.

In the author's view, what is the relationship between reflection and knowledge after reflection?
A) Knowledge is created by God and may or may not occur after reflection.
B) Knowledge is generated by the reflector and necessarily occurs after reflection.

Key Takeaway
The author reconciles divine agency with causal necessity: knowledge after reflection is from God and its occurrence is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If knowledge after reflection is necessary—as the author argues—does your practice of seeking knowledge reflect that necessity, or do you treat it as optional? What would change if you truly believed that reflecting deeply is the cause that must produce knowledge?
?
The author disagreed with both the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila on key points. Are you willing to hold a position that satisfies neither side of a debate if it represents the truth? Which matters more to you: being correct, or being aligned with a group you admire?

False Reflection Necessitates Ignorance
Al-Razi argues that false nazar does necessitate ignorance, against the majority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that false reflection (nazar fasid) can necessitate ignorance, and how he defends this view against a symmetry objection.

Core Idea
False beliefs can force a false conclusion, making ignorance necessary.

This lesson tackles a debate among theologians: does false reflection produce ignorance? The majority say no, but Al-Razi argues the opposite. He gives a concrete example to show how two false beliefs can logically lead to a third, and then addresses a clever objection using a symmetry argument. Follow his reasoning to see how conditional belief in premises resolves the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: statement of issue, author's view with example, objection, response.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'نظر فاسد' and 'جهل'.
2
Identify the two positions
The majority (including Mu'tazila) deny that false reflection necessitates ignorance. Al-Razi asserts it does. Locate these in the text.
3
Work through the author's example
The example: someone believes the world is eternal and whatever is eternal is independent of a cause. Ask yourself: what conclusion necessarily follows? Al-Razi says it's the belief that the world is uncaused – which is ignorance.
Focus on the logic: two false premises lead to a false conclusion.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The opponent uses a symmetry argument: if false reflection on a doubt produces ignorance, then a correct thinker reflecting on that doubt would also get ignorance. Al-Razi counters with an analogous symmetry about proof and knowledge. Then he introduces the condition: the effect depends on believing the premises are true.
This is the crux of the debate. Understand how the condition of 'belief in truth of premises' neutralizes the objection.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Al-Razi's answer shows that the same reflection can have different outcomes depending on the mental state of the thinker. This is why false reflection can necessitate ignorance for some but not for others.
Consider how this principle applies in real arguments: premises matter.

Source Text

مسألة : النظر الفاسد لا يولد الجهل ولا يستلزمه عند الجمهور منا ومن المعتزلة وقيل : إنه قد يستلزم وهو الحق عندى لما أن كل من اعتقد أن العالم قديم وكل قديم مستغن من المؤثر فمع حضور هذين الجهلين استحال أن لا يعتقد أن العالم فنى عن المؤثر وهو جهل . احتجوا : بأن النظر في الشبهة لو استلزم الجهل لكان نظر المحق في شبهة المبطل يفيده الجهل . جوابه : أنه معارض بأن النظر في الدليل لو أفاد العلم لكان نظر المبطل في دليل المحق يفيده العلم ، فإن جعلت هناك شرط الإفادة اعتقاد حقية تلك المقدمات فهو جوابنا عما قالوه
Issue: False reflection does not generate ignorance nor necessitate it according to the majority of us and the Mu'tazila. And it was said: Rather it necessitates, and that is the truth in my view, because whoever believes that the world is eternal and whatever is eternal is independent of an effecting cause, then when these two ignorances are present in the mind, it is impossible that he does not believe that the world is free of an effecting cause, and that is ignorance. They argued: If reflection on a doubt necessitated ignorance, then the reflection of the correct thinker on the doubt of the false one would produce ignorance. The answer: It is countered by the fact that if reflection on a proof yields knowledge, then the reflection of the false thinker on the proof of the correct one would yield knowledge. If you stipulate there a condition of benefit, namely belief in the truth of those premises, then that is our answer to what they said.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage addresses whether false reflection (nazar fasid) generates or necessitates ignorance. The majority opinion (including the Mu'tazila) denies it. The author asserts his own view that it does necessitate ignorance, giving an example: someone who believes the world is eternal and that whatever is eternal is independent of an effecting cause will necessarily conclude that the world is free of an effecting cause—a false conclusion, i.e., ignorance. The opposing argument uses symmetry: if false reflection on a doubt produced ignorance, then the correct thinker's reflection on the objector's doubt would also produce ignorance (which is undesirable). The author counters with an analogous symmetry: if true reflection on a proof produced knowledge, then the false thinker's reflection on that proof would produce knowledge (which is absurd). The resolution is that the benefit (or effectiveness) of reflection is conditional on belief in the truth of the premises. Thus, the objection is neutralized.

Key Terms
نظر فاسد
جهل
استلزام
شبهة
دليل
محق
مبطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Al-Razi denies false reflection can ever cause ignorance.
Correction: Al-Razi actually affirms that false reflection does cause ignorance in certain cases, as shown by his example. He argues against the majority who deny it.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Razi argue that false reflection necessitates ignorance?
A) Because false premises always produce false conclusions.
B) Because when two specific false beliefs are present, the conclusion follows necessarily.
C) Because all reflection produces either knowledge or ignorance.
D) Because the mind is compelled by any reflection.

Key Takeaway
False reflection can necessitate ignorance when false premises are believed, as shown by Al-Razi's example and defense against the symmetry objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What false premises about God or the world might you be holding without realizing it, and how could they be leading you into ignorance about the truth?
?
When you encounter doubts about your faith (shubuhat), do you honestly examine whether you are secretly believing the false premises behind those doubts? What would it take for you to reject those premises at the root?
?
The correct thinker remains unaffected by false doubts because he does not accept their premises. Are your convictions strong enough that false arguments cannot take root in your heart, or are you vulnerable because you entertain doubts without examining their foundations?

Does Faulty Reasoning Entail Ignorance?
Imam Razi discusses whether faulty reasoning yields ignorance and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's position that faulty reasoning can entail ignorance, and how he defends it against a symmetrical objection.

Core Idea
Faulty reasoning can entail ignorance even if it does not generate it.

Imam Razi tackles a nuanced problem: does engaging in flawed reasoning lead to false beliefs? The majority say no, but Razi argues that it can, through entailment. This lesson examines his reasoning, an objection, and his sharp response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two opinions
First, read the Arabic and translation to see the two main views: the majority says faulty reasoning neither generates nor entails ignorance; Razi says it entails ignorance.
The majority includes both Ash'aris and Mu'tazila here.
2
Understand Razi's example
Razi gives a concrete case: if someone believes the world is eternal and every eternal is independent of an effecter, these two false beliefs force the conclusion that the world has no effecter – a third ignorance.
Notice how entailment works: the new ignorance follows necessarily from the first two.
3
Follow the objection and its structure
The objection uses symmetry: if faulty reasoning entailed ignorance, then a correct thinker considering a false opponent's doubt would gain ignorance – absurd.
This is a common kalam move: show unwelcome consequences.
4
Analyze Razi's response
Razi counters with mirror symmetry: if correct reasoning yielded knowledge, a false thinker considering correct proof would gain knowledge – also absurd. He explains that the benefit depends on belief in the truth of premises.
Thus the objection is neutralized, and his view stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : النظر الفاسد لا يولد الجهل ولا يستلزمه عند الجمهور منا ومن المعتزلة. وقيل : إنه قد يستلزم وهو الحق عندي لما أن كل من اعتقد أن العالم قديم وكل قديم مستغن من المؤثر فمع حضور هذين الجهلين استحال أن لا يعتقد أن العالم فنى عن المؤثر وهو جهل. احتجوا : بأن النظر في الشبهة لو استلزم الجهل لكان نظر المحق في شبهة المبطل يفيده الجهل. جوابه : أنه معارض بأن النظر في الدليل لو أفاد العلم لكان نظر المبطل في دليل المحق يفيده العلم، فإن جعلت هناك شرط الإفادة اعتقاد حقية تلك المقدمات فهو جوابنا عما قالوه.
Issue: Faulty reasoning does not generate ignorance nor entail it according to the majority of us and the Mu'tazila. And it is said: it does entail, and that is the truth in my view, because anyone who believes that the world is eternal and that every eternal is independent from an effecter, then with the presence of these two ignorances it is impossible that he does not believe that the world is devoid of an effecter, and that is ignorance. They argued: if faulty reasoning entailed ignorance, then the reasoning of the correct person in the doubt of the false person would give him ignorance. Its answer: it is countered by the fact that if reasoning in the proof gives knowledge, then the reasoning of the false person in the proof of the correct person would give him knowledge. If you stipulate there the condition of benefit being belief in the truth of those premises, that is our answer to what they said.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the relationship between faulty reasoning and ignorance. Imam Razi begins by stating the majority view (including himself and the Mu'tazila) that faulty reasoning neither generates ignorance nor necessitates it. Then he reports a contrary view—that it does necessitate ignorance—and declares this as the truth in his own view. He provides an example: if someone believes the world is eternal and that every eternal is independent of an effecter, then with these two false beliefs, it is impossible for him not to also believe that the world has no effecter, which is a third ignorance. Thus faulty reasoning entails ignorance. Next, he presents an objection: if faulty reasoning entails ignorance, then a correct thinker considering a false opponent's doubt would acquire ignorance—which is absurd. Razi answers by countering: if correct reasoning yields knowledge, then a false thinker considering a correct proof would acquire knowledge—also absurd. He explains that the objection is symmetrical, and the answer is that the benefit of reasoning depends on the belief in the truth of the premises. For the correct thinker, the premises are true, so he gains knowledge; for the false thinker, the premises are false, so he gains ignorance. Thus his original claim stands.

الجهل
الاستلزام
التوليد
الشبهة
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faulty reasoning has no effect at all on one's belief system.
Correction: Razi shows that faulty reasoning can entail additional ignorance, even if it does not directly generate new false beliefs.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's preferred view, what is the relationship between faulty reasoning and ignorance?
A) Faulty reasoning generates ignorance.
B) Faulty reasoning neither generates nor entails ignorance.
C) Faulty reasoning entails ignorance.
D) Faulty reasoning only generates ignorance but does not entail it.

Key Takeaway
Faulty reasoning can lead to ignorance by entailing additional false beliefs, even if it does not directly produce them, as shown by Razi's example and his response to the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly that might be based on a false premise. What would it take for you to examine that premise honestly? Will you do it today?
?
Razi says faulty reasoning can entangle you in further ignorance. Are you willing to ask someone you trust to point out flaws in your thinking, even if it hurts your pride? When will you ask?
?
This lesson shows that even sincere reasoning can lead to error if the starting points are wrong. What are you building your worldview on? Are you sure those foundations are true, or have you just never questioned them? Resolve to test one foundation this week.

Knowledge of Proof's Indication vs Knowledge of Conclusion
Razi analyzes whether knowledge of proof's indication equals knowledge of conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that knowledge of how a proof indicates its conclusion is distinct from knowledge of the conclusion and cannot be the cause of it.

Core Idea
Knowing how a proof works depends on already knowing the conclusion.

This lesson unpacks a subtle epistemological issue in kalam: Is knowing how a proof indicates (wajh al-dalala) the same as knowing the conclusion? Imam Razi clarifies that there are three distinct types of knowledge involved, and shows that the relational knowledge of indication actually presupposes the conclusion, making circularity unavoidable if it were the source of that knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to get an overview of Razi's argument.
This is a dense argument; read slowly.
2
Identify the three types of knowledge
Razi lists three things: knowledge of the proof itself, knowledge of the conclusion, and knowledge that the proof indicates the conclusion. Find each in the text.
3
Understand why relational knowledge is distinct
Razi explains that knowledge of the relation (indication) is distinct from knowledge of the two relata because a relation is separate from the things it relates.
This is a key philosophical point.
4
Follow the circularity argument
Razi argues that if knowledge of indication were the source of knowledge of the conclusion, it would require prior knowledge of the conclusion, leading to a vicious circle.
This is the core of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة اختلفوا في أن العلم بوجه دلالة الدليل على المدلول هل هو عين العلم بالمدلول أم لا. والحق أن هنا أمورا ثلاثة: العلم بذات الدليل كالعلم بإمكان العالم، والعلم بذات المدلول كالعلم بأنه لا بد له من مؤثر، والعلم يكون الدليل دليلا على المدلول. أما العلم بذات الدليل فهو مغاير للعلم بذات المدلول ومستلزم له، وأما العلم يكون الدليل دليلا على المدلول فهو مغاير أيضا للعلم بذات الدليل والمدلول لأنه علم بإضافة أمر إلى أمر والإضافة بين الشيئين مغايرة لهما فالعلم بها مغاير للعلم بهما. ولا يجوز أن يكون المستلزم للعلم بالمدلول هو العلم يكون الدليل دليلا عليه لأن العلم بإضافة أمر إلى أمر يتوقف على العلم بالمتضايفين فالعلم يكون الدليل دليلا على المدلول يتوقف على العلم بوجود المدلول، فلو كان العلم بوجود المدلول مستفادا من العلم يكون الدليل دليلا عليه لزم الدور وأنه محال. وبالله التوفيق.
Issue: They disagreed about whether knowledge of the manner of the proof's indication of the conclusion is the same as knowledge of the conclusion or not. The truth is that here are three things: knowledge of the essence of the proof, such as knowledge of the possibility of the world; knowledge of the essence of the conclusion, such as knowledge that it must have an effecter; and knowledge that the proof is a proof of the conclusion. As for knowledge of the essence of the proof, it is distinct from knowledge of the essence of the conclusion and entails it. As for knowledge that the proof is a proof of the conclusion, it is also distinct from knowledge of the essence of the proof and the conclusion because it is knowledge of the relation of one thing to another, and the relation between two things is distinct from them, so knowledge of it is distinct from knowledge of them. And it is not permissible that what entails knowledge of the conclusion is the knowledge that the proof is a proof of it, because knowledge of the relation of one thing to another depends on knowledge of the two relatives, so knowledge that the proof is a proof of the conclusion depends on knowledge of the existence of the conclusion. Thus, if knowledge of the existence of the conclusion were derived from knowledge that the proof is a proof of it, circularity would follow, and that is impossible. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether the knowledge of the proof's indication (wajh dalala al-dalil) is identical to knowledge of the conclusion. Imam Razi presents the correct view: there are three distinct types of knowledge: (1) knowledge of the proof itself, e.g., that the world is possible; (2) knowledge of the conclusion itself, e.g., that the world needs an effecter; and (3) knowledge that the proof is a proof of the conclusion—i.e., awareness of the relation of indication. The first two are distinct, and the first entails the second. The third is also distinct because it is relational knowledge, and relations are separate from the relata. He then shows that the relational knowledge cannot be what entails the conclusion, because it depends on prior knowledge of the conclusion (since one must know the conclusion exists to know the proof indicates it). If it were the source of that knowledge, circularity would result. Therefore, the relation is not the cause of knowledge of the conclusion.

Key Terms
وجه الدلالة
الدليل
المدلول
الإضافة
المتضايفين
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that understanding how a proof works (i.e., knowing the indication) is sufficient to know the conclusion.
Correction: Razi shows that knowing the indication actually presupposes knowledge of the conclusion, so it cannot be the source of that knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi argue that knowledge of the proof's indication cannot be what entails knowledge of the conclusion?
A) Because knowledge of the indication is identical to knowledge of the conclusion.
B) Because knowledge of the indication depends on knowledge of the conclusion, leading to circularity.
C) Because the proof's indication is irrelevant.
D) Because the conclusion is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a proof's indication is distinct from knowledge of the conclusion and cannot be its source because it depends on prior knowledge of the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because you can explain an argument for God's existence, you truly believe in Him? Is your faith based on knowing the proof or on a direct knowledge of the conclusion in your heart? What would change if you moved from proof-based certainty to existential certainty?
?
Razi warns that knowing how a proof works depends on already knowing the conclusion. In your own life, are there areas where you are trying to prove something you haven't truly accepted? For example, do you try to convince yourself of God's mercy by reasoning, while your heart remains distant? What step can you take today to acquire that prior knowledge of the conclusion?
?
This lesson shows that relational knowledge (knowing that A indicates B) is distinct and dependent. Is your knowledge of Islamic proofs merely academic—knowing the relationship between evidence and belief—or does it flow from a living certainty of the conclusion? Which type of knowledge do you most need to strengthen right now, and what is one concrete action you can take to pursue it?

The Relational Nature of Indication in Kalam
Razi's commentary on the distinction between knowledge of indication and knowledge of the indicated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of the indication is not identical to knowledge of the indicated, and how theologians debate the nature of inferential knowledge in arguments for God's existence.

Core Idea
The indication is a relational entity distinct from both evidence and conclusion.

In this passage, Imam Razi comments on a prior argument about the nature of 'indication' (wajh dalala) in theological reasoning. He clarifies that knowing the indication is not the same as knowing the conclusion. The comment then critiques a potential flaw in that reasoning and connects it to the classic kalam argument from contingency. This lesson unpacks these layers of dialectical commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of Razi's commentary come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins by restating the author's intention: to show that knowledge of the indication is not identical to knowledge of the indicated. Find this claim and understand its significance for the argument.
3
Analyze the critique
Razi points out a flaw: the author's argument that relational knowledge depends on knowledge of the two relatives does not suffice to prove that it cannot entail knowledge of the indicated. He uses the analogy of cause and effect: an effect is derived from its cause yet still entails the cause.
This shows the nuanced dialectic in kalam.
4
Apply to theological proof
Razi then contextualizes this debate among theologians who argue from the existence of contingent things to God's existence. Understand how the question of indication's nature affects this proof.
The theologians ask whether the indication can be different from both evidence and indicated.
5
Note the answer and preview
The passage concludes with a proposed answer: knowledge of the indication is something existing externally. This will be elaborated later in the discussion of correlation.
This anticipates further discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : يريد أن يبين أن العلم بوجه دلالة الدليل على المدلول ليس هو عين العلم بالمدلول فبين أن العلم بإضافة أمر إلى أمر متوقف على العلم بالمضافين وهذا يكفيه ، ثم إنه أراد أن يبين أن الأمر الإضافي ليس هو المستلزم للعلم بالمدلول ، واحتج بأنه متوقف على وجود المدلول فلا يكون مستفادا منه وهذا البيان غير موافق للدعوى ، لأن المعلول مع كونه مستفادا من العلة مستلزم لها ، فإن أراد نفي تخصيصه بالاستلزام فليس في البيان ما يفيد ذلك . واعلم أن هذه المسألة إنما تجرى بين المتكلمين عند استدلالهم بوجود ما سوى الله على وجوده تعالى . فيقولون: لا يجوز أن يكون وجه دلالة وجود ما سوى الله على وجوده مغايرا لهما، فإن المغاير لوجوده داخل في وجود ما سواه والمغاير لوجود ما سواه هو وجوده فقط . بوجه دلالة الدليل على المدلول الذي هو مغاير لهما هو أمر اعتباري عقلي ليس . والجواب عنه : أن العلم موجود في الخارج كما سيجيء في تحقيق التضايف .
I say: He intends to show that knowledge of the indication of the evidence upon the indicated is not identical to knowledge of the indicated. So he showed that knowledge of the relation of one thing to another depends on knowledge of the two relative things, and this suffices him. Then he intended to show that the relational matter is not what entails knowledge of the indicated, and he argued that it depends on the existence of the indicated, so it cannot be derived from it. And this explanation does not accord with the claim, because the effect, while being derived from the cause, nevertheless entails it. So if he meant to negate its specification with entailment, there is nothing in the explanation to indicate that. And know that this question only arises among the theologians (متكلمين) when they infer from the existence of other than God to His existence. They say: it is not permissible for the indication of the existence of other than God upon His existence to be different from the two (i.e., the evidence and the indicated), for that which is different from His existence is included in the existence of other than Him, and that which is different from the existence of other than Him is only His existence. The indication of the evidence upon the indicated, which is different from the two, is a rational conceptual thing, not. And the answer to it: that knowledge exists externally, as will come in the investigation of correlation.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (أقول) on the previous discussion. It begins by restating the author's intention: to show that knowledge of the indication is not identical to knowledge of the indicated. The commentary then points out a potential flaw: the author argued that relational knowledge depends on knowledge of the two relatives, but the commentary says that this alone is insufficient to prove that relational knowledge does not entail knowledge of the indicated. It notes that an effect can be derived from a cause yet still entail the cause. The commentary then shifts to a specific application: theologians debate this when arguing from the existence of contingent things to God's existence. They ask: can the 'indication' be different from both the evidence and the indicated? If it were different, it would be a rational consideration. The answer given is that knowledge of the indication is something existing externally, which will be discussed later. This passage illustrates the nuanced dialectic in kalam about the nature of inferential knowledge.

Key Terms
علم بوجه دلالة الدليل
الأمر الإضافي
المتضايفان
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the indication (wajh dalala) is identical to the conclusion (madlul), i.e., knowing the evidence directly gives knowledge of the indicated without an intermediary.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the indication is a separate relational entity that exists between the evidence and the indicated, and knowing it is distinct from knowing the indicated itself.

Checkpoint
In the commentary on the question of indication, what problem does the commentator raise about the author's argument?
A) It is circular
B) It contradicts previous statements
C) An effect can be derived from its cause yet also entail it
D) The argument ignores the role of reason

Key Takeaway
In kalam, inferential knowledge involves a distinct relational entity (the indication), and the relationship between cause and effect complicates the argument that relational knowledge cannot entail the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the indication truly entails the conclusion, why does your heart sometimes hesitate to fully affirm God's existence despite clear proof? What in your life creates a barrier to that entailment?
?
Are you personally content with merely knowing that God exists, or do you strive to make that knowledge a living reality that transforms your actions? What step will you take today to move from knowledge to conviction?

Classification of Evidence by Imam al-Razi
Razi classifies evidence into three types based on the nature of entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's threefold classification of evidence into rational, transmitted, and composite, and the logical relationships that define each category.

Core Idea
Evidence is classified by whether entailment is rational, transmitted, or mixed.

Imam al-Razi begins by establishing the fundamental classification of evidence (dalil) and its relation to the indicated (madlul). He identifies three categories: purely rational, purely transmitted, and composite. The rational category is further subdivided based on the direction of entailment. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how proofs operate in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a sense of the three main categories without analyzing deeply.
Notice the technical terms: dalil, madlul, luzum.
2
Identify the rational subtypes
Look for the different inference patterns under rational evidence: one-way entailment (conditioned from condition) and two-way entailment (cause-effect, effect-cause, effect-effect, correlate-correlate).
These rely on whether entailment holds from both sides or only one.
3
Understand the condition for transmitted evidence
Razi states that purely transmitted evidence is invalid unless its truthfulness is first known by reason. Reflect on why this is necessary.
This shows the primacy of rational verification in kalam.
4
Reflect on the composite category
Composite evidence combines rational and transmitted elements. Razi says it is 'apparent' (zahir). Consider what he means.
Composite evidence is straightforward because it relies on both reason and report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المدلول ، وكل واحد منهما إما أن يكون عقليا مجنا أو سمعيا محضا أو مركبا منهما ، أما العقل فلابد وأن يكون بحيث يلزم من وجوده وجود المدلول، فاللزوم حاصل لا محالة من هذا الطرف ، فإن لم يحصل من الطرف الطرف الآخر فهو والاستدلال بالمشروط على الشرط كالاستدلال بالعلم على الحياة ، وإن حصل من الآخر فهو الاستدلال بالعملة المعينة على المعلول المدين والمعلول المعين على العلة المطلقة أو المعينة إن ثبت النساوى بدليل منفصل أو أحد المعلولين على الثانى وهو مركب من الأولين أو بأحد المتلازمين على الآخر كالمتضايفين ، أما السمعى المحض في حال لأن خبر الغير مالم يعرف بالعقل صدقه لم يفد ، وأما المركب فظاهر
The indicated (al-madlūl), and each one of them [the proof and the indicated] is either purely rational (ʿaqliyyan mujannan) or purely transmitted (samʿiyyan maḥḍan) or composite (murakkaban) from them. As for the rational, it must be such that from its existence the existence of the indicated follows necessarily, so the entailment (luzūm) necessarily occurs from this side. If it does not occur from the other side, then it is inference by the conditioned (al-mashrūṭ) from the condition (al-sharṭ), like inferring life from knowledge. If it occurs from the other side, then it is inference by the specific cause (al-ʿilla al-muʿayyana) from the specific effect (al-maʿlūl al-muʿayyan), or by the specific effect from the absolute or specific cause, if the equivalence [between cause and effect] is established by a separate proof, or by one of two effects from the other—which is composite from the first two—or by one of two correlates (al-mutalāzimayn) from the other, like the correlatives (al-mutaḍāyifayn). As for the purely transmitted, it is invalid, because the report of another, unless its truthfulness is known by reason, is not beneficial. And as for the composite, it is apparent.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies evidence (dalīl) and its relation to the indicated (madlūl). The author states that each of the two (proof and indicated) can be purely rational, purely transmitted, or a combination. For rational evidence, entailment (luzūm) must be from the proof to the indicated; if entailment goes only one way, it is inference from the conditioned to the condition, e.g., from knowledge to life. If entailment goes both ways, it is inference from cause to effect or effect to cause (if equivalence is established), or from one of two effects to the other (composite), or from one of two correlates to the other (like correlatives). Purely transmitted evidence is invalid because a report's truthfulness must be confirmed by reason first. Composite evidence is self-evident in meaning. The passage establishes the foundational framework for how proofs operate in kalam.

سمعي

علة
معلول
متلازمان
متضايفان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that transmitted evidence (e.g., reports) is automatically valid and does not require rational confirmation.
Correction: Razi clarifies that purely transmitted evidence is invalid because a report's truthfulness must first be established by reason before it can be beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the condition for purely transmitted evidence to be valid?
A) It must be from a reliable source.
B) Its truthfulness must first be known by reason.
C) It must be composite with rational evidence.
D) It must be based on a cause-effect relationship.

Key Takeaway
Razi's classification shows that rational entailment is fundamental to all valid evidence, and transmitted evidence depends on rational verification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you accept any claim about your faith without rationally verifying its basis, are you not relying on purely transmitted evidence which the author says is invalid? What specific belief do you hold that you have not rationally examined? Will you commit to examining it today?
?
The passage shows that even cause-effect inference requires a separate proof of equivalence. When you make judgments about Allah's actions based on worldly causes, do you have that separate proof? What assumption might you be making that you need to verify?
?
The purely transmitted category is invalid unless truthfulness is known by reason. On what rational basis do you accept the truthfulness of the Quran? Have you verified it, or do you blindly accept? Write one concrete step you will take this week to rationally verify a claim you currently take on authority.
